MEDLOW (Blue Mountains).

adjoining, and its level land proves it to be emine tly
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to I. ve
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PAI K,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICK Eli
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large —from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis, Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MEDLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the First Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any' cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on’this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly show's that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal

embraces diseases which probably cause more un
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.

The Co-Operative
SYSTEM, the nearest approach to Socialism that pan he achieved under the
present conditions of life, has met with phenomenal success wherever it has
been applied to any object. In England, even the Cooperative Societies
benefit some millions of persons—-their membership exceeding a million and
a-half, and hold invested capital to the amount of eleven millions of money.
This success is owing to the fact that the investor sees not only a fair com-
mercial return on his investment, but a proprietary division of profits, which
he would not receive from any other organisation. It has been left to Aus-
tralia to apply this communistic principle to
Irrigation
and the CO-OPERATIVE IRRIGATION and MERCANTILE SOCIETY
of AUSTRALIA, Ltd., have already secured three irrigation colonies in
Victoria, which ai-e partially settled and planted, and are negotiating for the
purchase of a tract of magnificent irrigation country in the West. Nego-
tiations are also pending for a Government concession of Murray land, and
there are now
Colonies
half-populated, awaiting the small Capitalist, the employed ARTISAN, or
the man whose sole .CAPITAL is his LABOUR.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 22, 1891.
Electoral Reform.
The New South Wales Parliament has now
been in session five weeks, and during that
time it has accomplished nothing, save the
passing of one small Bill on a private night.
The political kaleidoscope of which Premier
Parkes is the showman has presented a-
fresh combination of fragments to the ob-
server. Four years ago Freetrade was the
predominant feature. During the latter part
of the last Parliament, Federation was
shaken into the foreground. Now, however,
Freetrade has been-jostled out of sight, and
Federation is barely perceptible, while Elec-
toral Reform has been allotted the premier
position. There seems every present pros-
pect of Electoral Reform sharing, in its turn,
the fate of its predecessors, and, after
serving to divert the electors for a while,
being shuttled out of view to give place to
some other combination. The history of Sir
Henry Parkes’ management of the affair,*
so far, gives assurance that he has no serious
interest in the business. Although, for the
past twelve months, he has been protesting
that his , Government is consumed with
anxiety to give legislative sanction to the
principle of One-man-one-vote, it has become
obvious beyond mistake that the subject has
scarcely occupied his thoughts. At the time
when the last Legislative Assembly had an
opportunity of legalising the principle as
embodied in Mr. Traill’s plain and straight-
forward Bill, which, where defective, could
easily have been amended, the Government
and its hangers-on veiled their repugnance to
the principle under a pretence that they
were eaten up with anxiety to give it full
effect by adding a provision to secure the
franchise to electors of the nomadic class, such
as shearers and sailors. They dwelt upon this
desideratum with desperate iteration. Even
when they had to face their constituents
they adduced the absence of any provision
to effect this as their excuse for throwing
out Traill’s measure. But a lurid re-
flection is cast upon the sincerity of
Parkes and his accomplices by a discovery
which; the present Assembly has just made
In the “comprehensive measure” that>Sir
Henr y has so long been incubating there is
no provision whatever for securing the exer-
cise of the franchise to the nomadic class .of
electors, and it is essentially a Bill designed
rather to disfranchise the average elector
than to remove the predominance of the
plural voter,
* * *
Sir Henry Parkes has confessed that his
share hi tire construction of his long-talkcd-
of and much-vaunted Bill was confined to
jotting a few heads on a sheet of notepaper,
and handing that scrap of writing to a Bill-
drafter for expansion. The course of events
amply corroborates this confession. From
the first formal step in connection with the
draft it became perfectly apparent that Sir
Henry had never even glanced at the Bill.
At the very outset it was discovered that the
title was inadequate to cover the contents,
and the Bill was no sooner presented to Par-
liament than it had to be withdrawn and
hurriedly re-introduced. Then the Premier’s
second-reading speech was so brief, so
scanty, and so bald a performance that the
first ill-impression was deepened, and finally
when the Bill, after a too-superficial de-
bate on its second-reading, came to be con-
sidered in committee, it broke down at
the very second clause which was submitted
to examination. Two entire nights were
occupied in the endeavour to lick that form
less and incoherent clause into shape, and in
the end Parkes was glad to be permitted to
withdraw it for the purpose of having it re-
cast altogether ! If this clause were the only
faulty one in the Bill, that single imperfec-
tion would bo sufficient to indicate that a
singular indifference had attended its pre-
paration. But the entire measure is cast in
a frame similarly defective, and its very
essence is one of disfranchisement,
* * *
During the earlier stages of the discussion
Sir Henry Parkes apparently considered
himself safe in assuming an attitude of
swagger. He would be content, he declared,
to accept such amendments as might serve to
render the Bill more effectual, but he would
regard as censure of the administration any
alteration which should affect the essential
principles embodied in the measure. Now,
it is customary to regard as essentials such
principles as are defined in the title of a
Bill, Among those which find a place in
the title of this measure is the “ensuring
and giving due effect to majorities,” a phrase
which clearly refers to the system of
second elections, as adopted in France and
elsewhere, and which can, in fact, refer to
nothing else in the draft. The provisions to
this effect were evidently flung into the Bill
without mature consideration. They are
merely a badly-executed copy of a crude
and ill-digested scheme propounded by Sir
Samuel Griffith in the Queensland Parlia-
ment. And now that Griffith has aban-
doned his propositions, Parkes, by a
singular coincidence, admits that he also will
not do anything very desperate should this
great essential be struck out of his Bill,
• # *
But already there are tokens that further
demands will be made upon the docility
which has succeeded to Parkes’ former
martial attitude. Having consented to with-
draw for remodelling the clause which
brought the Assembly to a standstill last
week, the ugly consequence may ensue that
nearly half the succeeding clauses will, in
turn, have to be surrendered and re-cast, to
bring them into correspondence with the
alteration in the earlier one. In addition to
this, he will have to submit to the introduc-
tion of whole sheaves of new clauses and
new provisions, to enable the voter who
may, at election-time, be distant from the
electorate in which he is enrolled, to exercise
the franchise. Already there have been
submitted, for pre-consideration by the
Assembly, whole foolscap pages of fresh
clauses, designed to meet those requirements,
and these the N.S.W. Premier will either
have to swallow with as little grimace as pos-
sible, or to supplant by amendments of his own
to the same effect. His Bill, in short, is so
crude, so ill-considered, that scarcely any part
of it is likely to be accepted in its present
form ; and,- considering the amount of time
which has already been consumed in the
endeavour to knock the few opening lines
into some sort of shape, it seems improbable
that the 151 clauses which the measure
comprises can be dealt with without exhaust-
ing the whole session, and the patience and
endurance of the Legislature as well.
V .'\v v\ # # •
yParkee’much be-trumpeted Electoral Bill
is; in, truth',-a sorry jumble. It embodies no
dbmqeratic progression, save that which
Mr. Traill’s Bill would, if passed in the last
Parliament, have given in a direct and
simple fashion. ' Registration is indeed pro-
vided,- but in so curious a fashion that, in
place of affording assurance of a large
popular vote, 1 it would operate so as to dis-
franchise a large proportion of persons who
under the existing system have always
exercised the right of voting. Besides that,
the whole Scheme is so entangled with
another measure, the Redistribution of
Seats Bill, which has not yet even been seen
by members of the Assembly, that the one
will be useless without the other ; and mem-
bers already complain that while dealing
with the Bill in hand, they cannot really
appreciate the full effect of anything they
may determine. Beyond these features, the
draft itself is a mass of confused and
involved incongruities—a jumble of- compli-
cated machinery, , every wheel of which
seems likely to require remodelling and
readjustment in order to adapt it to altera-
tions which must necessarily be made to
repair the defective action of the parts first
dealt with, and to fill up the vacancies where
important functions have not been provided
for.
# # #
Were it the case that this pretentious Bill
embodied a number of really important and
novel principles' * the multiplicity of its
clauses and the intricacy -of its text might
be condoned, and even justified. But in view
of thq fact that all tins wilderness of words
is expended to effect a minimum quantity of
structural change in the electoral system of
the province, it is no wonder that discontent
swells. The science of electoral reproseata-
tion is fairly reasoned out, but its practical
application has yet nowhere made consider-
able advance. The system which has during
the past 20 years prevailed in New South
Wales has been an exceedingly primitive
one. After every general election the press
partizans of every shade of opinion make it
a custom to subject the results to an analysis,
more or less rough and ready. Their con-
clusions never by any chance agree m any
single respect save one, namely, that the
representation has scarcely any real corres-
pondence with the intention of the electors.
In one constituency members are returned
who have received only a minority of the
votes polled. In others a small preponderance
of votes enables a majority to send to Parlia-
ment three and four members, while a
minority numbering nearly as many as the
majority are altogether unrepresented.
Again, the ablest public men, to whom tens
of thousands of electors would gladly give
their suffrage, are liable to be excluded from
Parliament altogether, because one particular
constituency, in which the election is timed
among the latest, prefers some hero of the
pump and town-clock order. Everyone
of these is a genuine defect, and there is
not one which is irreparable, or for which
some effectual remedy has not been indicated
by impartial and philosophic investigators.
Yet, despite the immense pretensions of the
New South Wales Premier as to his promised
“Comprehensive Bill” not one of these
defects has been grappled with by its pro-
visions.
# * *
The Bill’s bulk is expanded by trumpery
provisions to permit of electors who shift
from Balmain to Ultimo, still exercising the
franchise, and in re-enacting, sometimes in a
less efficient fashion, conditions which already
exist in the law which the new one is
to repeal and replace. Every provision
which is novel in this province has already
found a place in the statute-book of some
other, and is simply borrowed therefrom
by grand old Prigster Parkes. There is
consequently in this draft Bill no feature
which will entitle its author to claim credit
either for wide scope of knowledge,
or for the courage of an originator.
The scheme is hopeless and incurable chaos
from first to last, and it would probably,
therefore, save much valuable time to at
once throw back the Bill to its author with
injunctions to bring in another decently
framed. And if such a course should involve
the displacement of the Ministry responsible
for so flagrant an imposition on the people
and Parliament of New South Wales, it will
matter very little. The Electoral Bill of
next Ministry must be better, for the suffi-
cient reason that it can’t be worse.
Relics.
ANOTHER “HOLY COAT.”
London, August 15. —The Roman Catholics of Argen-
teuil, near Versailles, intend to exhibit a “ Holy Coat ” on
the 16th instant, notwithstanding' that the Pope has
declared that the “Holy Coat” of Treves is the only
genuine one.— Cablegram.
On the small but ancient and charmingly
situated city of Treves, on the Moselle, there is
at the present moment converging a huge army
made up of recruits of all lands and languages.
A peculiar feature about this advancing host is
that room is found in its ranks not alone for
females, but for the lame, the halt and the
blind, as well as the young and the old of both
sexes. They are pilgrims bent on viewing the
coat worn by the Saviour of mankind on the
eventful Friday morning nineteen centuries
ago, when the rabble, with the permission of
the Roman authorities, crucified the man whose
advanced socialism, in religious as well as in
secular matters, was offensive in their eyes.
All that is now known of Jesus of Nazareth
is contained in the four canonical gospels.
These four were chosen out of about 50 by the
Council of Nice in the early part of the fourth
century. The Council consisted of 318 bishops,
most of whom could not read. Indeed,
B alin us, one of their number, observed that,
with the exception of two, they were “ simple,
illiterate creatures.” It is impossible to say
now how the assembled divines chose the four
true accounts and condemned the false 46. It is
recorded, however, that the whole 50 were put
under the table, and that the present quartette
—Matthew, Mark, Luke and John —at once
jumped on -the table. This is probably un-
trustworthy.
* # *
Towards the end of the third century St.
Helena, the mother of that Constantine who
saw the fiery cross in the sky, became a Chris-
tian, as well as her son, and established a
reputation for piety. To attain such a reputa-
tion in those days does not appear to have been
so difficult as in these. Among other actions
in favour of her new creed was a journey made
by her to Jerusalem for the double purpose of
discovering the sepulchre of the Saviour as well
as the cross on which HE suffered. She found
both. But she found much more. The astute
people of Jerusalem, in view of what, without
irreverence, might be called the Western “run
on relics,” were not backward in finding them
to order. With a reverential spirit that did
her credit, St. Helena wished to discover if any
of the garments worn by the Crucified on
Calvary were still preserved. It was quickly
found that many of them (after a lapse of 300
years) were in a fair state of preservation. In
fait, so many were produced that a fresh diffi-
culty at once arose. It was hard to say with
certainty which were the true ones. That all
could be genuine was out of the question, unless
on the blasphemous assumption that the Con-
demned was in business, and carried his stock
to the place of execution. At last St. Helena,
guided no doubt by supernatural advice, chose
a seamless coat as the one worn on the cross.
It is this coat that is preserved in the Cathedral
of Treves—it is to see it that pilgrims in mil-
lions are now on their way across mountains,
continents and oceans.
This is the place for a few words concerning
relics in general before returning to the Holy
Coat of Treves. They are common to all
religions and to all nations. They satisfy a
deep-seated craving in the human mind— -a
craving which, kept within legitimate bounds,
is highly creditable to humanity. But it never
has been, is, or will be kept within legitimate
bounds. At Mecca is preserved the stone whose
eventful history was told to Abraham by an
angel. The stone might be removed and
another substituted, but the only result would
be a probably greater number of pilgrims and
a probably higher percentage of cures. So
with Buddha's tooth at Ceylon. It might be
stolen or ground to powder, as it once was,
but there is little doubt that a fresh tooth
would be found possessing all the charac-
teristics of its predecessor. When millions
of Buddhists demand a grinder as a relic of
Gaudama, it is certain that a grinder will be
found for them. The Romans exhibited frag-
ments of the good sl\ip Argo, which bore the
apocryphal Jasor and his apocryphal comrades
on their eventful but problematical voyage. In
the Epistle to the Hebrews, Paul speaks of the
Holy of Holies, which contained ‘ ‘ the golden
censer and the ark of the covenant overlaid
round about with gold, wherein was the golden
pot that had manna, and Aaron’s rod that
budded.” But when the Roman, Pompey, sacri-
legiously stood where none but the High Priest
had previously presumed to enter, he found-
nothing.
* # #
The Saxon monarch, Athelstan, presented
many powerful relics to the monastery at
Exeter—among others, the candle lighted by
the angel of the Lord in Christ’s tomb, the
burning bush from which Jehovah spake to
Moses, and one of the stones used in the
martyrdom of St. Stephen. Edward the
Confessor, of England, and Louis IX., of
France, were likewise great collectors and
generous donors of the upper and lower ex-
tremities of long-departed saints. Why any
physical or mental ailments should have been
permitted to exist in European countries, con-
sidering the ease with which they might be
cured by a visit to the nearest shrine, has never
been explained. Still, it was reserved for the
Crusaders to bear the markets by the relics
sent West in shiploads after the sack of
Jerusalem, at the beginning of the 13th cen-
tury. From Constantinople to Cadiz there was
such a boom in bones, that even the smallest
village shrine could boast of tibise or fibuke
whose former owner had long ceased to have
any further use for them. A liberal discount
was allowed to families and schools, until
the West became a repository for the articula-
tions of the East. Bearing in mind the character
of the people who swarmed to the Crusades,
when their native lands were becoming too cir-
cumscribed for their enterprise, the ungenerous
suspicion quickly arose that most of the relics
sent from Palestine had been obtained from the
nearest church-yards.
* # #
The people of to-day are too apt to assume in
their arrogance that their ancestors of the 12th
and 13th centuries were a good deal behind the
present times. In many things they un-
doubtedly may have been ; but it is question-
able whether the smartest trader of the present
would have had so much to boast of when it
came to a bargain for a consignment of skulls.
It would be too much to suppose that no
mistakes were made in the relics before the
Reformation came and introduced a chronic
slackness into the business. Many replicas
were sold, so that while a German church re-
joiced in the possession of a whole skeleton,
another skeleton of the same person might be
found curing congenital infirmities on the other
side of the Rhine. Perhaps the most familiar
case is that of the two crowns of thorns, one of
which was secured by a certain monastery, while
a neighbouring establishment obtained the
other. That both were genuine was so obviously
improbable that many a head was broken in the
quarrel as to which was the true one—a
question that remains undecided to this day.
How it has come about that no rival coat to the
one at Treves has secured a footing in religious
circles [since this was written there has
been received the cablegram which appears
above], or that no further garments were dis-
covered when the success of the latter was
assured, is not very plain. By such remissness
an opportunity was lost which is not likely to
recur unless, indeed, Baron Hirscii and his co-
religionists succeed in forming colonies on the
Jordan, when, from the proverbial ingenuity
of the Jewish people and their acquaintance
with the clothes-line in all its branches, further
discoveries may be anticipated with confidence.
Having said so much of relics in general it is
time to return to the subject of the Holy Coat
of Treves.
# # «S
We have no record of the number of pilgrims
who went to view this memento when it was
exposed a few years ago. But, so far back as
184-1, no fewer than a million and a-half of
people visited the shrine on the occasion of that
year’s pilgrimage. Now that the means of
communication have been so multiplied all the
world over it may well be that an equal, or even
greater, number will take advantage of the pre-
sent opportunity. At the same time the spread
of scepticism has more than kept pace with the
spread of railways and of shipbuilding. 1 11
fact, it would not be too much to say that every
fresh locomotive or telegraph-line heralds an
addition to the ranks of secularism. It IS-
therefore, with some surprise we learn that
already thousands of Americans are on their
way to Treves. If one could be certain that the
pork-merchants of Chicago, the lobbyists o!
Washington, or the contractors and civil ser-
vants of Canada would return new men, the
surprise would quickly give way to satisfaction.
Still, it is to lie feared that on the North
American continent, as elsewhere, the Old
Adam finds too congenial a shelter in mens
hearts to be banished by the sight of a coat
whose chief characteristic appears to be the
absence of seams. Besides, if the Yankees am
Canadians wish to inspect relics, they need not
go far from home. In Mexico and in South
America the followers of Cortez and Rizahi-o
took care to supply the various churches "dn


relics that had seen much service and effected
many miracles in distant Spain. Many of these
souvenirs had done good work against the
Moors, and might fairly be warranted to keep
up in the New World the reputation they had
acquired in the Old. It is probable that the
Incas, with many mementoes of their own holy
men, would not care to sell their country for
boxes of foreign vertebrae. But the far-seeing
Spaniards brought blunderbusses as well as
bones ; and the fact is now beyond dispute that
a relic with a musket is worth the contents of
a vault with bows-and-arrows.
* * *
So far as Australia is concerned, it may
be asserted with considerable confidence
that no movement on either a small or
large scale will be made in the direc-
tion of visiting Treves. A few Australians
who happen to be in Europe may be tempted
by curiosity to mingle with the crowd, and a
few may even be induced to attend from pious
motives. But the State School system has done
its work. Secular education and a Holy Coat
are a badly-yoked couple and decline to go
together in harness. This big Island of ours
views with gentle cynicism everything in the
relic line, whether it be the arm of an anchorite
of the uniform of a prince. Big as Australia is
there is no room in it for fairies, ghosts, saints,
regalia, or relics. In fact, there is little room in
it even for idle clergy. Already the churches
are demonstratively forsaken by men, and
nearly so by women and children. A constant
dribble of ecclesiastics has to be kept up from
the other side of the world, as the material for
their manufacture is almost entirely absent
among us. The idea of a born Australian
appearing at Treves to inspect the Holy Coat
is too grotesque to be dwelt upon without a
generously-capacious smile.
* * *
It is not, however, the religious world
alone that invites derision by its faith in
the authenticity and virtues of holy garments
and holy bones. There are secular as well as
sacerdotal relics. The crowns, sceptres, regalia,
coronets, robes, garters, pumps, and breeches
appertaining to monarchy and aristocracy are
as vulgar in conception and as potent for mis-
chief as the contents of all the saintly sarco-
phagi of the old world. They are relics of a
barbarous past when violence and wrong held
undisputed sway—when the Western European
nations, like the Russians of a few years ago,
consisted of but two classes, masters and slaves.
Nor, although daily falling into disrepute, have
these tawdry insignia yet wholly lost their
potency for evil. Millions still reverence or
affect to reverence them, and their baneful
influence has reached even far Australia.
Under their protection there has grown up
amongst us a class of frowsy upstarts whose
ignoble ambition it is to perpetuate, in new
lands, the titular absurdities of old ones.
Again, our lawbooks are relics, not alone of
mummery, but of cruelty. On the Statute
Books of every Australian state at the present
moment are laws which are daily put into
execution, and which would libel a society of
Thugs. It was but last week that, at Bathurst,
a couple of men, made presumably in the image
of Gon, were ordered to be bound while a. large
portion of their trunks were being skinned.
After a month or so, when nature had supplied
fresh cuticle, it was to be again removed by a
succession of blows, and so on till one wretch
had been tortured twice and the other three
times. Yet the lawmakers and the journals
that uphold this vivisection have the cool
audacity to prate about Siberian cruelty or
Oriental insensibility.
Widows and the State.
There is a mixture of good and bad in Mr.
Ninian Melville’s proposal to impose a tax on
every ton of coal raised in New South W ales,
to be applied in relieving the families of men
killed in coal-mines. Every extensive mining
accident creates a widespread feeling of public
sympathy, and provokes a considerable amount
of more or less ostentatious alms-giving. The
women and children whom the calamity robs of
their bread-winners are generally in some way
provided for by public subscription. They
become the pensioners of a charity fund. This
is a good thing in so far as a fund is brought
into existence for the purpose ; it is objection-
able in so far as the fund is tainted with what-
ever of humiliation is implied by the so-called
charity. The best feature of the proposed
legislation is that, while not abolishing the pro-
vision for those bereaved, it would put it on a
different footing. The widows and the father-
less would be fed, but not with the bread of
charity. Whatever help was given them they
would take not as a matter of grace, but as a
matter of right. The wife of John Jones,
deceased coal-hewer, might collect her monthly
dues from the public treasury with as indepen-
dent a spirit and as high a head as the daintiest
civil servant drawing his salary, or a retired
Chief Justice his pension.
# # *
Another advantage of the measure would be
the doing away with unequal and spasmodic
distribution. At present the amount of assist-
ance given to any individual depends entirely
upon the circumstances of the particular acci-
dent that necessitates the assistance. The
woman whose husband is killed in a great catas-
trophe like the Bulli explosion may find herself
in receipt of a little income for life, while, if he
had been the solitary victim of a fall of coal,
the most she could expect would be a small
contribution to relieve her immediate distress.
And yet her wants arq none the less pressing
when she has no companions in misfortune than
if every woman in the district had been made a
widow at the same moment. Public charity
never proceeds on rational grounds. The
motive that should actuate anyone in contribut-
ing to a relief-fund is that certain individuals in
want of food and clothes have no means of
getting them. That motive is never sufficient
to produce much result. The public cannot be
roused to enthusiasm by the mere intel-
ligence that some uninteresting individuals
a hundred miles away are hungry and
ragged. They want stirring. Let a hun-
dred men be blown into eternity without
a moment’s warning, and you get the necessary
sensation. Subscription lists can be circulated
at once, and they will be filled. People give
their money, not because so many women and
children have lost their husbands and fathers
and been rendered destitute, but because the
husbands and fathers departed this life to a
sufficiently dramatic accompaniment. To adopt
Melville’s resolution would be to change this.
The distribution of the relief-fund would pro-
ceed on the ground that the death of a bread-
winner had made relief necessary, not that the
circumstances of the death were exciting
enough to supply the evening papers with a
page of sensational cross-headings.
* * *
But the weakness of the proposal is that it
does not go far enough. It still indirectly
recognises the fallacious distinction between two
classes of the helplessly destitute as objects of
public concern—between those who owe their
unfortunate position to fatal accident, and those
who have come to grief in any other way. No
such distinction can logically be drawn. If it
is a proper and a praiseworthy thing to make
public provision for the widow and the orphan
of any man who may be killed by an explosion
of fire-damp, there can be no valid reason for
withholding the same relief from the family of
the man who comes to his death by con-
sumption or typhoid. The former case
is the one that attracts public attention
and sympathy. The public mind is always
open to be moved by startling tragedy
in actual life or on the stage. It appeals to the
dullest imagination. And yet perhaps the most
pitiable case is the commonest, where the
breadwinner is struck down by mortal disease,
and dies after a lingering illness, which leaves
those dependent on him not only without means
of support, but overwhelmed with hopeless
debt.
* * *
The question for the legislature is whether
the weak and helpless have any claim against
the community at large which the community
is willing frankly to recognise and meet. If so,
the action taken should be not piecemeal, bvit
comprehensive and thorough. But if public
opinion is not yet ripe for that, then I he Bulle-
tin will welcome even a fragmentary instal-
ment. The step once taken, will never be
retraced. It is the direction in which we shall
march boldly in the future, when the eyes of
the race are opened to see that even as a mere
matter of business it will pay the State to take
under its charge the weaklings —children too
young to earn their bread, and old men whose
earning days are over—and the women, for
whom the race has other work to do.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Chinaman and the Nigger.
The other evening, when Parkes’ new Elec-
toral Bill rose like a midnight spectre in the
N.S.W. Assembly, Gar van arose, armed with
an inspiration. He wanted to know particularly
whether Parkes had provided a vote for the
Australian aboriginal; and following him came
Houghton, who went the whole legislative hog
by demanding that the suffrage should be
granted to the naturalised Buddhist, so that
the yellow man might be an elector and a
brother, and one Chinaman might have one
vote. So far as the aboriginal was concerned,
Sir Henry viewed the proposition with favour,
but he drew the line at the Buddhist. The
old, coloured owner of the soil has enjoyed a
vote in the past under certain conditions, and
Parkes declined to tear the ballot-paper away
from the last wrecks of a decaying race. To
disfranchise him would be “ a lasting disgrace
to this democratic land, and, in his mind’s eye,
the old man evidently saw a tearful picture of
the native shivering in a tree on the summits of
a thousand hills, a broken-down black Avreck,
damp, naked and insolvent, but hanging on to
his vote as the one consolation that remained
to him on earth. Sir Henry seldom shows
to better advantage than when he is looking
after a distressed female, or propping up a
dying race, or shedding the tear of sensibility
over bones, and poems, and ruins, and sodden
graves, and similar relics; but even he might
have been puzzled to tell Avhat use a vote is
to an aboriginal, and why it would be a dis-
grace to any democratic country if he hadn’t one.
A vote is a means whereby a citizen expresses
his independent opinion on public matteis, and
if he won’t and can’t express an independent
opinion, then he ought not to have a vote, foi
lie only uses it as somebody else’s dummy, and
resurrects the old plural-voting iniquity once
more. The blackfellow has no opinion worth
mentioning, but that difficulty could be got
over, seeing that any number of white men are
in the same situation; the one hopeless
obstacle is that, as soon as he learns anything
of civilisation, he gives up being independent
then and for ever. It is his pride and joy to be
Mr Hangar’s blackfellow, or Jack Hamil-
ton’s nigger, or Billy Rutledge’s aboriginal;
the idea°of being his own aboriginal never enters
his head. His constant desire is to have a
patron who will keep him in tobacco and occa-
sional drinks, and he will vote for his patron
just as every other man will vote for the party
which fills his political programme. The
arrangement suits both parties concerned, ihe
big landowner who owns the nigger is the same
landowner who wants to own the white man,
but in default of the white man the nigger-
provided he has a vote—does almost as well.
Nobody can own the nigger except the land-
owner, for the coloured gentleman always gives
his allegiance to the person avlio lends him a
tree to roost under, and therefore the
arrangement is equally good for the
big capitalist and the undressed native.
But it is good for no one else. !No
amount of pathos can get over this obstacle
—the blackfellow doesn’t want to be an inde-
pendent elector, and won’t be one on any terms.
Even a democratic country doesn’t give the
suffrage to infants and horses, for such a pro-
ceeding would only give a plural vote to the
infant’s male parent and to the man who drives
the horse ; and the aboriginal occupies precisely
the same situation. An adult nigger without a
vote is an anomaly in a free country, and so is
an adult Chinaman in the same situation : but
the anomaly can’t be helped, and Mr-
Houghton’s attempt to draw a parallel between
the naturalised German and the naturalised
Mongol or the native-born black, is supreme
folly. The naturalised German can become an
Australian citizen in all essentials of citizen-
ship ; the Mongol and the nigger remain
Mongols and niggers to the bitter end. More-
over the picture of these forlorn pilgrims pining
for their lost suffrages and refusing to be com-
forted is too thin. The nigger doesn’t want a
vote, apart from the question of rum and
tobacco, any more than the black snake wants
a new hat; he has no idea—of his own —what
to do with it; and he has no desire to become a
legislator, and would much prefer being a
policeman on account of the club and the
uniform. And, anyhow, a ballot-box is a hard
substance with which to console a dying race,
and if the nigger is dying it is more to the point
to let him down easy with grub and blankets
and tobacco. And as for the Mongol, the pro-
posal to give a vote to the poll-taxed heathen
who is about to be kicked out may be theoretical
justice, but as he won’t remain long enough to
enjoy it, the proceeding is superfluous and
various other things as well.

The Lost Bore.
Alfred Allen, poet, M.L.A. of Paddington
(N.S. W.), was visited the other day by an
inspiration. He conceives that a legislator is
paid for attending Parliament, arid therefore
his screw should be stopped on those nights
when he isn’t present. This looks, on the face
of it, a reasonable proposition, but it won’t
bear inspection, and nobody, except Alfred
Allen, could have struck such a piece of weird
and solemn foolery. For, in the first place, a
member is not paid for his attendance ; he is
paid partly to legislate and partly to block
other people’s legislation, and he must be
judged entirely by results. The mere tact that
a man has sat hard down in the House all
through a session of six months is not, of neces-
sity, any more use to the country than if he had
sat on damp grass for 60 years in an uninhabited
land. If a man appears once in a year or so
with a noble and convincing idea to enunciate,
he is of more service than Alfred Allen
ever will be, seeing that he merely talks
till he takes root, and never has any
ideas at all. Mr. Allen has slopped over
for years, with a ceaseless flow of thin, washy
platitudes, and yet George Reid, in his single
speech on the necessity for law-reform in Aus-
tralia, did more real work than the other man
could do if he lived to be the Last Man of all,
and made continuous orations all the time. The
House, in fact, wants to pay some men to be
present every night, and some other men to
keep away perpetually, and Mr. Allen is one
of the other men. It is impossible to
run Parliament on Sunday-school bun-struggle
principles. If it insisted on all the bores being
present all the time it would only be jawed to
death. If it insisted on the good men being
present all the time they would be technically
present but practically absent; in other words,
they would sign the roll, and then fly to the
refreshment-room to escape the bores who had
been rallied up by Act of Parliament. The
scheme might work in a silent Parliament
where every member merely sent in his speech
to Hansard, and offered no audible observations.
Or again, it might work in a Parliament run on
the pantomimic principle of the new French
worldless play of Pierrot and Pierrette, if such
a thing could be devised. Then there would be no
speeches at all, either verbal or printed, but
each senator on rising would hang a card on his
front button, explaining in large print the sub-
ject which he wanted to discuss, and would
express himself by dumb show and gesticula-
tion, and by the indignant firing out of his arms
and legs in various directions, and by his per-
spiration, and the glare in his sinister eye, and
the standing up of his hair, like the quills of
the indignant porcupine. Or, again, as a last
alternative, the House might allow every
member to make a speech on his first appear-
ance, and then if he proved a confirmed and
incurable bore it could briefly cork him up, and
make him confine himself to mere voting for
ever afterwards, on the ground that he had been
sampled and had proved a failure. On any of
these three principles it would be feasible to
compel the constant attendance of all members,
but not otherwise. There are about 98 hard,
irredeemable nuisances in the New South Wales
Legislature, and when they appear the windows
of Heaven are opened and the great deep of
bosh is broken up, and it rains ineffable crazy -
ness day and night. And if they were all there
constantly, and the senate never got a rest,
their blood would be upon their own heads.

“ Unearned Increment.”
A workingman’s wife, writing to a Sydney
paper, tells how she and a friend were swindled
by a couple of the financial dodges to which
the law gives the dense shelter of its silence.
Her friend, about to marry, put down £35
towards buying a little cottage and land, and
engaged to pay 12s. 6d. per week until the
balance of a fixed amount was extinguished.
He paid away regularly for 18 months, and was
then legally ordered to clear out, as the man
who sold him the property had gone insolvent,
and the official assignee had taken over all
his property, including the house and
land. That is a story you often hear.
A man able to rent a “flash” office in
Sydney or Melbourne may start as a landed
proprietor any day and do business with the
uninstructed and credulous public. The legend,
“Caveat Emptor,” is decipherable over every
commercial door, and is answer in full to all
ordinary complaints. The purchaser of land or
of a house should make sure of his title, and if
he pays his money without doing so he must
take the risk. Thus is the question settled on
the side of the buyer. The seller has, however,
still to be dealt with. “ Caveat Emptor ” is,
in his respect, a mere license to cheat. The
law does not formally ratify this license. Now
and again it condemns the whole thing. But
what it should do, not now and again, but
always, is to make it a criminal offence, punish-
able by penal servitude, for any man to sell
what he does not fully own. The workingman’s
wife also puts forward the grievance that, after
paying for a certain time into a society, she
loses all because she had fallen into arrears.
There is a world of pathos in that statement.
The working classes are literally fleeced by
individuals and companies who calculate on the
incidence of calamities among weekly wage*
earners—want of employment, or sickness, < r
accident—intervening as the years go by, and
leading to the confiscation of the payments
made. The Legislature has here a field of rank
imposture to attack. Under the guise of
encouraging thrift there exist persons, a main
element of whose income is the payments made
by wage-earners during the period of their em-
ployment, and forfeited by them afterwards on
account of non-employment or of sickness or of
some other distress beyond control. There is
plenty of unearned increment apart from that
of land.

A Comprehensive Clause.
When the Police Offences Act Amendment Bill
came up for consideration in the Victorian
Council, Hon. James Service rose to demand
the insertion of this brief but comprehensive
clause :
Where any person is accused of bein'' an idle or dis-
orderly person on the ground that he has no visible
lawful means or insufficient lawful means of support, the
fact that he can produce or prove that he possesses
money or property shall not be taken into account in
deciding the charge against such person unless he is able
to show that he honestly obtained such money or pro-
perty.
Truly the old man of Property who sketched
out that clause was wise in his day and genera-
tion. When the modern “ cop,” acting in the
interests of Capital, comes across anybody who
is hungry, or ragged, or homeless, or out of
work, he is duly authorised to arrest him for
having no money and no food and no place to
sleep in. . He can be sent to prison for 12
months for having no cash about him, though
if he were accused of pounding his wife instead
of being hard-up, he would only get seven days.
The law takes no account of why he is hard up;
if it were properly administered in all its
pristine beauty it would lead to the arrest of
ship-wrecked sailors, and the sufferers by the
Melbourne floods, and all poor people who are
burnt out and have lost their clothes and their
furniture. There is one weak point about this
ancient statute, and only one—on all other
points it is as hard as cast-iron or frozen brass.
When a man is arrested for having no money,
and proves in coui’t that he really has money,
and that the indictment is a lie and the arrest-
ing constable an ass, he is generally allowed
to go —reluctantly, it is true, and with a
solemn warning not to do it any more,
but still he is allowed to go. This
is the matter which the Victorian Upper
House desires to remedy. The proposi-
tion of Mr. Service simply means that when a
man is charged with having no cash, and he
produces the cash and thereby gives the lie to
the whole accusation, he shall be sent to gaol
for having no cash all the same, on the off-
chance that the property was dishonestly
acquired. The law won’t attempt to prove that
it was dishonestly acquired —it will be author •>
ised to take the dishonesty for granted, unless
the prisoner can prove the contrary. If the
property—whatever it is—has been in his
possession for many years, and he can’t in-
stantly produce the man from whom he ob-
tained it, he will go to prison there and then.
He will get the customary three months, partly
for not having any assets when he ought
to have them, and partly for having
assets when it is presumed without any
evidence at all —that he ought not tb have them.
It is a clause which covers everything and rakes
in everybody, and the old capitalists of the
Upper House seem to have accepted the situa-
tion cheerfully. At least, nobody threw' any-
thing at the bloated landlord who proposed
this atrocious legal outrage, and nobody cast
him down the front steps, and no paper de-
manded his resignation as an incurable lunatic,
and he still flourishes and helps to make laws
for the Victorian democracy.

If Parkes’ Electoral Bill becomes law after
proper democratic amendments, The Bulle-
tin people will vote for the old man next
election—provided they have a vote in his
electorate. Several months ago, this paper
cordially invited Sir Henry to introduce a
measure providing that no member shall lie
elected to the New South Wales Assembly
unless he receives an absolute majority of the
votes polled, and in cases where no candidate
receives such a majority, the matter to be;
decided by a second ballot. It also promised
that if he did bring in and pass that measure,
The Bulletin would vote for the aged poet at
the first opportunity under the new system.
Possibly this lavish offer roused Parkes up, for
the required proviso appears in his Electoral
Bill, and if that document gets through safely
Sir Henry will receive more votes than he
expected next polling day. Possibly these
votes wdll pull him through, where he would
otherwise have been defeated, but The
Bulletin cordially trusts they may not.
The paper will blast Parkes with
its accustomed vigour both before and after
the election, and its sentiments tqwards
him will undergo no change. It will continue
to regard his poetry as hogwash of the worst
description, and to consider him a public fraud
of the first water, and it will still hold the fixed
opinion that his hair ought to be cut, and that
he wants a clean shave. It will look upon him
through all the ages of futurity as a person
with a shifty eye and an unreliable 1.0. U. But
if his Bill passes in its present form it will do
its best to give him the votes already men-
tioned, and it trusts that he wdll feel aggravated
at getting them. The Bulletin’s moral pro-
missory note has always been redeemed at
maturity, and if the old man only gets two sup-
porters altogether at the next election he will
know by this intimation where they came from.
This offer holds good under all circumstances,
and even if the great Hi-Ham is unseated pre-
maturely for insolvency, cannibalism, or con-
spiracy with a Chinaman, so long as he presents
himself on polling-day it makes no difference.

“ Loins.”
This is the history of the life of a once robust people
now scourged hy Protection written in blood, and its
aarlands of national life changed to the gloomy trappings
of national death.—Sydney E. News.
The wild, sad song of John Haynes —it must
1)0 John, for no other individual could be so
loose in his facts and so mad in his poetry—
thus bursts forth again in Sydney E. News,
about the decline and fall of Victoria under the
baneful influences of Protection. Also, it must
be Haynes some more because the writer
evidently doesn’t know that there are any other
places in the world except New South Wales
and Victoria, and he isn’t sure about Victoria.
The burden and lamentation of this little man
howling'in the wilderness is just this—owing to
Protection people don’t marry in Victoria, they
have few children, and they die off hurriedly,
while in New South Wales owing to Foreign-
trade they marry like blazes, and have lots of
children, and live to a green old age. “ Those
who are born wither like fragile flowers into
the grave ” moans tins prophet of evil, and
what became of those that are not born he
doesn’t mention. And again he breaks forth
in this awful manner :
Taken together the colony enjoying Protection which
was to make the people happy and prosperous was beaten
•’ y the ITeetrade colony by product from the loins of the
people by 07,804 souls in a run of only 13 years.
John always was great on the subject of
“ loins,” and the expression recalls the bright
days of the old, vanished past. And we pre-
sume it would be no good mentioning to John,
or to the News, that Victoria is ahead in the
matter of marriages per thousand of the popu-
lation, after all, and that Protectionist Queens-
land beats the New South Wales record by a
tremendous percentage. Also, it would be no
use at all to mention that in the matter of births
Queensland and W.A. both leave the Foreign-
trade Cornstalk in the rear ; and that in Maori-
land and S.A.—Protection notwithstanding—
the population doesn’t die off nearly so fast as
it does in the land of Botany Bay; and that,
in the natural increase of population, Maori-
land, which isn’t Freetrade by any means,
wipes out the whole lot. It is the thinly-
peopled country which shows best in the vital
statistics, and Protection has no more to do
with it than the transit of Venus has with the
price of salt fish. And, if people don’t die
much in New South Wales it is possibly because
New South Wales isn’t a good and pleasant
place to die in. Possibly the sight of Haynes
i.browing poetry off his chest in this awesome
manner has something to do with it; possibly
they are scared in their declining years by
John’s observations about “loins;” possibly it
is something else—goodness only knows what.
Anyhow, it doesn’t matter.
The 3rd clause in the original draft of Attorney-
General Simpson’s Criminal Law and Evidence
Amendment Bill, now before the N.S. W. Legis-
lative Council, recalls the innovation made by
Sir Alfred Stephen at the first trial of Henry
Louis Bertrand —an innovation which more
than all the other vexed points in the case
tended to save that individual’s neck. After
22 hours’ deliberation, without agreement, Sir
Alfred, the presiding Judge, at that time Chief
J ustice, discharged the jury and remanded the
prisoner. When, on a subsequent trial, Ber-
trand was found guilty, Sir (then Mr.) Julian
SALOMONseontended that Sir Alfred’s discharge
was had in law on the ground that the jury should
not have been released except on a medical cer-
tificate testifying that they could not go on
further. He contended that if a Judge could
discharge after 21 or 22 hours he could do
so after one hour, or any time he choose.
Although Bertrand was finally found
guilty, this contention of Salomons’ affected
the public mind and operated actively to post-
pone the death-penalty. Afterwards it was
fixed that a Judge could not discharge a jury
until after 12 hours’ inconclusive consultation,
and it is this practice which Mr. Simpson’s 3rd
clause originally proposed to abolish. He ex-
punged it, however, after reflection, and thus
the free discretion Sir Alfred presumed to
e.xercise over a-quarter of a century ago will
continue to be limited by law.
A S.M. Herald correspondent says that union-
ism has injured labour because “ those who
possess the capital to live without having labour
done, or doing it themselves for a livelihood,
will not invest their money, or spend it only as
it pleases themselves,” and from this remark-
able premise deduces the conclusion that the
lowering of wages would result in increased
opportunities for labour. For instance :
“ Some hundreds of landlords would he able to
have painting done if it was more reasonable.”
The gentleman’s reasoning is even more extra-
ordinary than his grammar. In the first place,
capital held out of use ceases primarily to he
capital, eventually to have any existence, simply
because it can only be replenished when used
productively hy labour. Then, the lowering of
wages would not necessarily produce reduction
in the price of commodities, hut would certainly
result in an increase of profits and a consequent
increase in the luxury of idlers and in the
number of those who are drones hy choice.
Lastly, if an approximation to starvation wages
would give “thousands of landlords” an
opportunity to “ have painting done,” a closer
approach hy the workman to the ohtainal of
ilie full results of his toil would enable millions
of wage-earners also to have “a little painting
done.” Higher wages mean a higher standard
of living, therefore, an increased expenditure,
consequently, a greater demand for labour.
Lower wages mean precisely the reverse. The
man who writes to Tory journals recommending
his fellows to accept low pay, is, despite his bad
grammar, too evidently a bogus toiler.
It was ex-Attorney-General Wrtxon who re-
solved the Victorian Parliament to strike out
the term, “ Commonwealth ” from the Federa-
tion Bill. It wasn’t his great speech that did
it, for the speech fell comparatively flat ; the
peroration settled the business. “ What,” he
demanded, in tones of woe and thunder, “ would
people think in Great Britain if Gladstone and
the Irish Party were to insist not only on
having Home Rule in Ireland, but on calling
the newly-enfranchised .State ‘ the Common-
wealth of I reland ?’ ” Thai paralysed the House,
and “Commonwealth” was struck out there
and then. Everybody was floored on the spot.
Not one member had ever looked at the matter
in that light before. The Legislature fell down
in a horror-stricken heap in the face of this
argument. And yet, after all, wdiat would
people think if the Commonwealth of Ireland
were started to-morrow ? Nine-tenths of them
wouldn’t think at all, because they had never
learned how to do it. The other tenth w r ould
think about their own business just as usual,
and wouldn’t mind whether Ireland was a
Commonwealth or not. There would be no row
or convulsion of any kind. Beer wouldn’t go
up. Nothing would happen. Nobody would
be killed. That is The Bulletin’s idea of
what people would think about the Irish
Commonwealth. What Wrixon thinks they
would think isn’t mentioned. That brilliant
loyalist merely threw up his hands in horror, and
in the name of all the dead kings that continu-
ally howl in Sheol, what are they expected to
think in such a case ? The whole thing reminds
us very much of another question that another
fool asked some 70 years ago or more. He
wanted to know what people would think of
being carried along in a train at the rate of 20
miles an hour. And all the people think is that
20 miles an hour is hideously slow. Meantime,
“ common-poverty ” is the title for Ireland,
viewed how you like.
For the first time in the Queensland Parliament
the Northern members are going to vote as
a solid bunch, the subject of their advocacy
being Griffith’s three-pronged division of the
colony into provinces as a substitute for com-
plete Separation. This really means apparent
victory for the Separatists, the price of the
Northern support being Griffith’s promise
that, if the Southern bunches should succeed in
casting out the Bill, he will himself go the
whole hog and no longer oppose Knutsford’s
action in the Separation matter. So, apparently,
if the Northerners win now, they lose to win
hereafter ; if they lose now, they win hereafter
all the same. We have italicised apparently.
Griffith was long known as “ Slippery Sam,”
and he always opposed Separation. Besides,
he may be only finessing with the Southern
contingent that threatens to split away from
him ?

Dear Bulletin, — This is the simple truth
about Maoriland’s position with respect to
Federation. Her contribution to the cost of
Federal Government would be, at the lowest
Convention estimate, £lBO,OOO per annum, and
her yearly loss of revenue by the adoption of
the Victorian tariff, £400,000. In other words,
she would have to pay £580,000 a year—for
what ? For the privileges of being in the
Commonwealth, of being outvoted and governed
by countries 1200 miles away who can’t
govern themselves and drop a million a
year each in trying, and of being de-
fended in time of war by a fleet of cock-
boats with more or less “ dicky” nervous
systems. They are a mean people these
Maorilanders, and they won’t do it. Hence
the tears of the Sydney Morning Herald,
which reminds Maoriland of the time when she
“ set a higher value on aid from Australia.”
Now as to that. When Maoriland v/as being
colonised, Sydney supplied the land-speculators
and rum-peddlers and head-hunters (there used
to be a big trade done in the heads of Maoris),
who made a little Sheol of their own at
Kororareka, then the principal settlement.
Sydney sent over a “Resident,” who incited
the artless Maori chiefs to run a Constitu-
tion of their own. Sydney found the
land-grabbers who went to Maoriland with
bottles of rum in one hand and ready-made
conveyances in the other ; and Sydney, after
doing more than was done otherwise to pro-
voke the natives, generously allowed Maoriland
to take a few regiments of Imperial soldiers,
who employed their spare time in painting the
settlers’ villages a lurid red. When Hongi,
the Maori Napoleon, returned from England,
loaded with presents from the nobility, who
bought those presents, and sold Hongi the
muskets and ammunition, with which he sub-
sequently carried out a comprehensive scheme
of human butchery and cannibalism ? Sydney !
Ungrateful Maoriland ! —Pakeha.
The Great Woman Question.
Dear Bulletin, —Premier Parkes 1 violent old-age love of
petticoats is making trouble. Too much sentimental
nonsense is talked about woman’s equality with man, her
surprising intelligence, and her ability to hold her own
in any sphere. The nature of the sexes at once capsizes
any such assertions. The one fact of maternity displaces
woman from all pretensions to equality with man. There
can be no privilege without a corresponding duty. If
women have the same political privileges as men, they
ought to accept the same responsibilities. Are they
willing to do this? Will lovely woman answer to the
bobby’s call, “In the Queen's name,” for a refusal to do
which a man can be punished ? The Queensland Defence Act
has provisions by which, stage by stage, every man Jack
in the community up to a certain age can be compulsorily
called out as a soldier; will lovely woman volunteer to
amazon to that roll-call ? Notice, again, the effect of
woman suffrage. Every voter can, by law, also become a
member of Parliament. Make a woman a “ votress ” and
she can illogically become a “membress.” Lovely, isn’t
it? Fancy Mistress Speaker dandling her last pair of
twins when ruling that the expression “nasty old cat’’
was strictly parliamentary ! Just fancy the Hon. the
Treasurer’s Budget speech being postponed on the certifi-
cate of an obstetric surgeon! Fancy Mrs. Bludanfire
becoming a captainess in the navy, or a Judgess of
the Supreme Court, and “ firing low and laying ’em out,”
or ordering a male culprit who had once wounded her
vanity, six dozen lashes, or to he hanged by the neck till
he was dead. Lovely woman, when she “ misses stays,”
won’t answer the helm. The one hope of preserving and
developing a healthy human race lies in keeping woman
to her womanly duties, in enabling her to live a natural,
as opposed to an artificial, life ; in preserving her nervous
system from all unnecessary strain, in relieving her
whenever possible from those particular forms of brain-
pressure to which men are especially subject. Physio-
logically speaking, in the absence of transmissible
■organic diseases such as phthisis, scrofula and so on, the
meanest, weediest creature that ever crawled on (lie
earth’s surface in human male shape may be the father
of offspring that can develope into healthy and strong
men and women, provided the mother lie a physically
fine animal. On the other hand, the most physically
perfect man abounding in vitality cannot hope to found
a healthy family—if any family at all—with a weedy,
high-strung, amende (though intellectual) wife. With a
few exceptions that merely prove the rule, experience
goes to show that specially brainy women (any more than
the most brainy men) are not calculated to become good
reproducers of their species. Under the present high
pressure life of modern civilisation and all-round competi-
tion, our young men “don’t feel well,” but if we place
young women in this whirling maelstrom of combat, and
they too begin to find that they “don’t feel well',’'it fr
idle to hope for the improvement or even maintenance of
the rape of humans., I believe that the strongest nation
is where the woman ispoliticaUy a cypher, where she is—
Ido not mean to be offensive—a healthy, happy mammal
—where, if she has to toil physically, her toil is of such a
nerveless nature that She is even improved physically and
in relation to maternal capacities. Take the German
nation, for example. lam English, but I have been in
Germany,as in other lands. I consider the Germans to be
physically the strongest nation in the world; yet they
produpe the greatest average of great original thinkers—
of philosophers, poets, litterateurs, scientists, strategists,
and soldiers. At one end of the German social scale you
find your Sohlegels, yOur Moltkes, your Schillers, your
Bismarcks ; at the. Other end you see the bulky, sullen
Bavarian emerging like a wolf from the forest to squelch
like eggshells the French. Yet in Germany woman is
nothing but what, from my point of view, she ought to be
—a wife, a mother, a domestic worker and overseer
(these spheres embracing all the realms of refinement in
which woman may advantageously shine); whereas in
France she is, at the very least, a shopkeeper, and her
upper-class influence has dominated French politics and
sapped French virility for at least two centuries. A
woman who pretends to intellectual superiority is
generally unlovely to man. No, sir ; lovely woman is just
about right where she is. Her weakness is her strength.
—Dipso.'

What is the matter now with the latest
big mining sensation, the Last Call Mine ? Those
who bought into this block of solid “ oof ” at £2 per
share (there are only 200,000 shares in all) are now
beginning to look very blue, as they see the stock
quoted at 6s. or 8s. Yet experts have visited
this Cloncurry wonder and verified its tremendous
worth. When we invest the balance of our shat-
tered fortune in a mining spec, it shall be in a
show condemned by all experts in general, and
reported as utterly valueless by the local warden.
A few months ago the Orange contingent
on and about Sydney Exchange rejoiced exceed-
ingly at a dividend on “Toms”—about the last
thing any sane speculator expects from the ordi-
nary N.S. W. raining spec. Hereupon the local
talent bought heavily of the scrip. “Sell your
shirt andhuy Toms,” was the cry, for were there
not three more dividends in sight? But, alas ! on
the top of the threepenny dividend came a shil-
ling-call, and a sick feeling came o’er the “ bulls,”
as the holders who had been willing to share a
good thing shot their scrip on top of a broken-
backed market regardless of price. And now the
holders of Toms Contributing don’t ask what you
will give, but what you will take to become a
shareholder; and fourpenee per share is freely
presented to anyone courageous enough to carry
the baby.
The new Tasmanian hangman—who has
taken over the business so long and ably con-
ducted by Mr. Solomon Blay, and hopes, by
punctuality and strict attention to the obliteration
of customers to merit a continuance, &c.—started
work the other day by strangling Mr. Cooley.
The new executioner was disguised in false hair
and red paint. Why he was disguised Heaven
only knows, but most likely it was because he was
ashamed of himself. Hobart has fallen off a lot
of late years, and whereas eggs was undoubtedly
eggs down there in the olden days, they aren’t
always eggs now. The “ System ” is dead, and
the hangman doesn’t shine in society as he once
did, neither does the gaol-fiogger move in the best
circles, as the man of past years used to do. It
was always thus. The hangman withereth, and
the flogger fadeth, and all things pass away.
The absolute disregard of truth (without
telling any lies) which Sir Henry Parkes can
exhibit at the briefest notice is strikingly illus-
trated in the following extracts from two speeches
delivered by him in N.S.W., within two months.
Both extracts are from N.S. W. Hansard
I maintain that the true
patriotic feeling which we
ought to cultivate is that
every distinction, every
honour that can fall upon
her Majesty’s subjects
within the United Kingdom
ought to be shared with the
colonists of Australia. I
think that peerages ought
to be offered to worthy
persons in the colonies, as
well as to worthy persons in
the mother country.—May
21, 1891.
I sat here, sir, and I heard
this gentleman (Sciiev)
deliberately state that I
had advocated a colonial
peerage. At no moment of
my life, by any single word,
did I ever support anything
of the kind, or entertain a
single thought of the kind,
and that is a sample of the
truthfulness of the hon.
member’s references to me.
—2lst July, 1891.
A squatter who applied for a reduction of
rent, told a pathetic story in the Sydney Land
Court, the other day. His establishment was
194 miles beyond Wilcannia, in a parched and
hungry district where everything was sun and
sand, and some of the sand was yellow, and some
of it was grey, and some of it was glaring white
sand, and it was all one great, hot, boundless
monotony. The man next door was 30 miles
away, and on the other three sides there was no
man next door at all. He got five inches of rain
on an average every January, and the rest of the
year there was not even a shadow. Water was
so scarce that he and his men generally washed
for about three weeks in the same jugful, and
then it would be inspected by a committee,
before it was thrown away, to make dead sure
that they couldn’t wash in it for a week longer.
When they did throw it away, it was like burying
a beloved brother. The wool had to be carted 194
miles over ghastly roads, and then it was shipped
from Wilcannia if there was water enough in the
rivet. There was a big dam on the run which had
absorbed most of his spare cash, and as
soon as it was finished some officials came
and surveyed a road right through it,
and Were going to let all the water out.
They had about 6000 square miles of elbow-room
to make that road, but they must make it through
his dam, and nowhere else. Therefore, he had
come 915 miles to lay Ids troubles before the Land
Court, and 915 miles back would make just 1830.
If he got a reduction of rent it would pay him for
the journey; if he didn’t he would at least have had
the pleasure of seeing a few strange human beings,
and having a wash in some place where there was
lota of water. He was a poor, thirsty squatter,
and life wasn’t worth having.

There are 15 wool-buyers, seven of them
new, on board the Oceanien for Sydney,
Sydney Echo, in advocating the lash for
“brutes,” points out the “presentment” on the
subject which a British jury lately made to Mr.
Justice Hawkins. The Echo, however, carefully
suppresses what Mr. Justice Hawkins said in
reply to the British jury aforesaid.
The Provident Society in connection with
the establishment of Messrs. Anthony Hordern
and Sons, held a social at Sydney New Masonic
Hall, last Friday. The proceedings consisted of
songs, recitations, violin solos, and various other
joyous items, and a dance followed. Everything
was a pronounced success, and the show ended
happily for all parties concerned. Mr. Harris
acted as manager and superintendent of things in
general, and filled the bill in a gratifying manner.
The Victorian Supreme Court having
decided to uphold the Judge’s ruling that a man
who knew the difference between right and
wrong could not be insane, and the Executive
having therefore ratified murderer Colston’s
doom, it is contemplated to bring before the
Court a number of madmen who admittedly know-
right from wrong and are yet confined in the
public asylums, and ask for their discharge. The
legal definition of insanity is, of course, 500 years
behind scientific facts.
A Sydney Sunday paper throws a column
and a-half of appalling language at Parkes, and
describes him, to put the matter gently and in an
abbreviated form, as being all the hogs, dogs, and
devils of this world rolled into one. In a general
sort of way it asserts that the giddy old man is all
the bad things it can remember, and all the bad
things it has forgotten as well. And now the
country wants to know—as it has often wanted to
know before, and as it never did know, however
much it wanted—why Parkes calmly stands this
sort of thing, instead of starting the largest libel-
action that ever raised the circulation of a paper’
Why is it?

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
