“The Idler,” a play founded on a lack of
acquaintance with the extradition laws, on re-
vival at Sydney Garrick, drew a big first-night
bouse. It is not so well played as previously.
Young Cosgrove has not the weight and
experience necessary to delineate the highly-
respcetable and titled M.P., who has been a
debauched gambler. Nelly Lyons, badly wigged,
is altogether misfitted as the gushy, husband-
hunting widow. Somehow, in the present pro-
duction, all the faults of the play are accentuated,
particularly those of the dragged-in comedy to
obtain whose lighting-up effects the author makes the
ingbnue hunt her father, a twice-widowed, mental
and physical ruin, even into bachelor chambers,
while the Yankee millionaire—a presumably
sombre Mafiaite, W’ho thirsts for the gore of his
brother’s murderer —fills up the intervals between
vows of vengeance in making idiotic love to the
sister-in-law of the object of his detestation.
Another jarring note is provided by the hinted at
engagement of the widowed and fatuous dilapi-
dation, aforesaid, to the Idler's mother, a com-
fortable widow in a high state of preservation,
mental and otherwise, although throughout she
seems to have looked on that social mildew after
the fashion of the Lady Benevolent, who
cheers the declining days of rheumatic in-
digence with chews of tobacco and flannel in rolls.
The Idler, who is “not a man of the world,” of
Chas. Cartwright is quite up to its old-time
excellence, though unnecessarily strident and
noisy at times, while counterbalancing the too
fatal fascination resulting from the donning of
better-cut pants by the putting-on of an infinitely
worse-shaped wig. Probably we shall have to
take a course of some very nauseous actress as an
emetic to rid us of reminiscences of Sara. Mean-
time, the only actresses that can be tolerated are
those who are satisfied to let the highly emotional
rip. Olga Nether sole doesn’t; that is probably
the reason that she scarcely meets with such uni-
versal approval as on her last visit. Cautley kills
his American by “dolling” him up and painting
him a mild terra-cotta hue. His love-locks and
mustivy demand such attention that little time
remains for anything else, and when he stepped out
of a band-box on to the stage for the first act he
seemed afraid to move lest something should give
way. Jenny Watt-Tanner and Mary Kingsley
are all right, and Harold Russell is little worse than
before. There is a deal of crude, young-man
cynicism in “The Idler,” though some of it, for
instance the line, “ The sufferings of one-half the
world are necessary to the happiness of the other
half,” fairly hits the mark. It may profitably fill
the bill throughout its destined fortnight,
** * *
“The Merry Wives of Windsor” hasn’t
staggered the Sydney public to any appreciable
extent at Her Majesty’s, chiefly because they
have stayed away in large masses. The perform-
ance has suffered thereby, in most instances; it is
hard to pump up enthusiasm to a chill and empty
house. George Rignold and his lady-assistants
seem least affected thereby, and Sterling Whyte’s
Bardolph is one of the best drunkards known to
the local stage. “ Bill ” Rignold’s pale-blue cap
and ribbons of the early acts sit too garishly on
his elephantine bulk, and in his ground wallow-
ings he is reminiscent of a stranded whale. The
irritating custom still prevails at this house of
taking “ calls ” before the curtain, dooming the
unhappy mummers to troop across like Richard-
son’s showmen. A raising of the curtain on an
extension of tableau seems the only artistic
method of response to exhibitions of popular
approval. Sims’ theatrical meteor has had a long
spell at this house, but his “ Lights o’ London ”
are turned up once more on Saturday night.
There will be an interregnum of four nights at
Her Majesty’s on 15th, 16th, 17th, and 18th Sep-
tember, between the leaving of the Go. for Mel-
bourne and the arrival of the Laura Villiers’
crowd.
** * *
At Sydney Royal business continues big
with “Old Guard,” in which Elton continues his
wild career of beer-drawing, shaving, and sentry-
go with much approval. Charles Ryley being
laid up with congestion of the lungs, Syd. Deane
plays Marcel, and Tom Grundy has stepped into
the thereby vacated shoes of Caramel. Whether
looked at vocally or verbally “The Old Guard”
cannot be accounted anything but an extravanga
of the “Evangeline” type. The latter having
been successful here, it was a foregone conclusion
that the other should go and do likewise. By the
way, we learn from a contemporary that “ the
gods ” have declared as a reason for encoring the
ballet that they’ve got to pay double and so
demand double dancing. The Gaiety Co. are due
on 12th September, and as soon as J. C. William-
son’s six months lease is up some alterations will
be made to the proscenium and a new act drop, a
Venetian scene by Geo. Gordon, will be hung.
** * *
Pattie Browne having retired with a
fractured knee-cap, Emma Temple' has filled her
place at Sydney Criterion during the last few
nights, and the latter lady’s post as Mrs. Bill
has been occupied by Nellie Mortyne. “ A Night
Off ” is revived on Saturday for a season of 11
nights. “Dr. Bill’s” revival biz. has been quite
equal to the primary season.
&* * *
At Coogee Aquarium Manager Larmour
intends to revive Saturday afternoon shows with
the return of warm weather, and future Sunday
afternoon concerts will be graced with a band for
lawn-promenading purposes.
** * *
W. H. Jude and his concert-lecture
matineed on Wednesday before a good audience
of mostly women. Mr. Judo is a single-handed
show (not in any way resembling Punch and
Jude) “under the patrpnage of the Governor and
Bishop,” and as he is usually faced by a congre-
gation and not an audience, his programme is full
of constantly-recurring clerical witticisms which
enrage the heathen portion of his auditors. Mr.
Jude must possess a wrought-iron gullet, for he
talks and sings for over two hours without becom-
ing throaty in the least. All his songs are “ little
things of His own,” and a new one, “Jack
Ashore,” ought to get a bid from loud, fierce
baritones who love to stand on the rolling deck
and all that sort of thing. His strong speciality
is the harmonium, on which he is certainly a
unique and marvellous performer.
* * * *
R. S. Smythe, having secured his two
celebrities, Stanley and Max O'Rell, has been
touring in Brittany and Normandy with Henry
Blackburn, the art-critic, who wrote two charm-
ing volumes about those pleasant bits of old
France wh’ch once formed portion of Old
England. The Much-Travelled is a very good
judge of pictures, and was the first to discover
that the so-called replica of Mrs. Butler’s famous
picture, which the trustees of the Sydney Art
Gallery purchased, was not really painted by her.
Smythe will remain in England to welcome back
Charles Clark,
* * * *
John Sheol (’L) Sullivan and his “ puggy
push” opened at the Opera House in the Mar-
vellous City last Saturday night before an audi-
ence mainly composed of the outpourings of the
Apollo Hall and other bruisers’ resorts. Up in
the gallery they noisily made their presence
known throughout. Owing to indisposition,
Sullivan the Slogger did not put in an ap-
pearance until the third act, consequently the
expectant public displayed considerable im-
patience of the tissue of trivialities and tauto-
logical trash with which the piece labelled
“Honest Hearts and Willing Hands” abounds.
Duncan B. Harrison is responsible for the
cobbling of this American absurdity, and has
apportioned to himself speeches as long as
those recorded by any phonograph. Dramatic
construction and coherency are not in it, and the
torrent of talk tails out to a lame and impotent
conclusion. Slogger Sullivan is a fine but flabby
person with a convex stomach, whose present con-
dition does not argue well for his chance with
Frank Slayin should he meet him. His acting,
though intended to be pathetic, partook of the
bathetic, and the audience roared with laughter at
his alleged sentiment. This irreverence aroused
the wrath of the podgy “ pug ” who crossed to the
prompt corner of the stage and said, more
suo : “ Some o’ yer will get a tail put on yer. Ain’t
yer got no respect for the awjence?” This
brilliant flight of oratory was vociferously ap-
plauded, and the angered giant bowed his
acknowledgments, then struck an attitude. The
boxing scene between himself and Jack Ashton
was a palpably rehearsed affair, although after
the second round Sullivan was winded and seemed
distressed otherwise. The verdict of the public
was anything but favourable, and the omens
are for a short-lived season. The Australian
public will not stand tommyrot dramas, and the
Slogger will have to jump into the prize-ring if he
w r ants to make money in this country. Between
the fourth and fifth acts Duncan Harrison made
an ill-advised apology to the audience for short-
comings. This kind of thing is now called
“ making a Villiers,’ 1 in view of recent appeals of
a like nature.
* # * *
“We Two” and “Woodbarrow Farm”
have been limping along at the Royal to unprofit-
able results, and a change of tactics is now an-
nounced. French melodrama is to have a show,
Burnand’s adaptation of “Une Cause Oelebre ”
being underlined for production. “ Peril,”
at the Bijou, has succeeded in drawing fair
attendance, and on Saturday next, “Devo-
tion,” by Dion Boucicault, junr., will be staged
for the first time in the colonies. The
piece had somewhat of a run in London
some years ago, nevertheless the Argus scribe
the other day wrote that this was the first drama-
tic essay of the youthful B. As a matter of fact
he has written several pieces,
* * * *
The London Gaiety Co. are still wrestling
with “Ruy Bias,” and owing to the illness of
Chas. Danby its announced successor, “Cinder
Ellen,” will not be placed on the boards till Satur-
day next. Fred. Storey has been advantageously
doubling Don Salluste with his own part.
* * * *
Opera at the Alexandra succeeds in a fair
to middling business manner. Madame Simonsen
decidedly had a bit up her sleeve in the Cutticas,
husband and wife. The former is an excel-
lent tenor of the robust order, and his wife a
cultured soprano. Her singing of the “ Last Rose
of Summer,” in “Martha,” knocked her hearers
and the non-hirsute Hopetoun put his hands
together for an encore the night he put in an
appearance. Madame Simonsen has gathered
together some very capable singers, and should
receive her reward by the patronage of the many.
The minor shows are all doing well ; Frank M.
Clark’s audiences, at the Gaiety, maintaining their
manifold attendance, the Victoria Hall receiving
good recognition, while Phil. Stuart smiles
serenely as he counts the Waxworks receipts.
* * * *
The writer remembers our late visitor, Mrs.
Abbey, playing Nan, in “Good for Nothing,” at
the little Margate (Eng.) Theatre. Probably
there never was another Nan who could so well
afford to wash her face before an audience and
look so fresh and rosy when she had finished
towelling. Then, and for some time afterwards,
the beautiful Florence Gerard (not Girard) was
serving her stage novitiate in Henry Neville’s
company. She played in the curtain-raiser when
“Turn of the Tide” was produced at the
Olympic, with George Anson and Mrs. John
Wood as Mr. and Mrs. Danby.
* * * *
The Victorian Orchestra breathed its last
in a miserable style at Hamilton Clarke’s benefit
concert. A large number of instrumentalists
evinced their devotion to their late leader, and
supplied further evidences of the beautiful
brotherly-loving influences of professional music
(particularly the “high-class” article) by not
turning up on the interesting occasion. It was a
fitting end to a long and weary attempt at proving
that the Victorian Orchestra, as conducted and
managed, was a source of public delight, instead
of tire hollowest of failures all the while. As a
matter of fact Hamilton Clarke did not shine as a
conductor. He has considerable merit as a com-
poser, though.
* * * *
As the Mariposa got alongside her berth
at Woolloomooloo somebody in the crowd yelled,
“Show us John L. Bring him to the front.”
Then the moonlight shone on a mass of butcher-
meat leaning over the rail, and, in a voice of bovine
sweetness, the distinguished visitor hailed the
shore with, “Hello, you bloomin’swine !” We
believe the observation was simply a friendly
greeting to an old pal. Anyhow, it’s the first
experience we got of the great artist who followed
the divine Sara at Her Majesty’s, Sydney.
** * *
It is funny to heat an actor criticise the
performance of a brother artist. He lays himself
out to find faults in the “ business,” the untradi-
tional exit, the way of “taking the stage,” the
everything that has no bearing upon the realisa-
tion of the character in hand. He can’t, in many
cases, understand that personal mannerism, mag-
netism, and “style” are worth a lot of salary to
a man who has to engage the attention of an
audience, quite unconcerned about what he
doesn’t so long as what he does is effective. The
actor-critic is shocked by the incapacity of a
G. 0. Miln, and smiles pityingly when the house
occasionally rises at such a raw amateur. Now,
the real, good sound sort of actor, according to the
nerfesh, is Jimmy Cathcart. “You can't place
iiim wrong,” they will say. Quite true, again.
The veteran is correctly mechanical in all
characters.
At Poverty Point.
Some of Lyster’s old chorus once again showed up in
Simonsen's. They beat the ballet tor lasting, and are
good, too, although familiars have disappeared, notably
the bassoonlike grandfather Benham, with his nasal pom-
pom ; and he came from the London Italian Opera of
1851, when poor Susini was the star basso there, in
“Ernani.” There used to be two little, old, fat fellows,
intensely Hebraic, credited with fish-hawking in the day
time, but one was a vocal ophecleide, and the other a
human trombone. Raucous youth is long growing to
this. Good job.

bourne prior, it is said, to leaving for dear hold Hingland.
Edith Blande joins Bland Holt for a Maoriland tour.
Coppin pioneered Melbourne opera with such as Cathe-
rine Hayes, Anna Bishop, Madame Cailly, Madame Caran-
dini, Sara Flower, Octavia Hamilton, M. M. Conlon,
Laglaise, John Gregg, and so on, and ended with his
famous dictum, “Operawon’t pay in Australia.” Madame
Carandini was the original here of the dashing bandit,
Fra Diavolo.
Sara Bernhardt had a rough trip to Auckland. The
Mariposa was hove-to in a gale for 48 hours.
There are to be amateur theatricals at Sydney Govern-
ment House on 27th and 28th insts.
Someone publicly reproved J. O. Williamson for favour-
ing Melbourne over Sydney in the matter of “ new pro-
ductions,” i.e., new so far as Australia is concerned
J.C.W. writes to say that Sydney, in past years, has
enjoyed the honour of various new productions, viz.,
“Pinafore,” “Pirates,” “La Mascotte,” “Merry Duchess,”
“Estrella,” “ Mikado,” and so on, and that but for certain
circumstances Sara Bernhardt would have opened in
Sydney city. He also avers that when he has a theatre
in the old city equal to the Princess of the new, that
original productions will be more fairly divided. The

The other day The Bulletin printed a paragraph stating
that Mr. Henry Edwards, who lately died in New York,
left no near relatives. Mrs. Elizabeth Elinor Edwards
now w’rites us from Point Piper Road, Woollahra, Sydney,
asking us to mention that she is the law’ful wife of Henry
Edwards’ brother William, who is supposed to have died a
few years since in Queensland, and who has three children
living. Any exact information concerning the death of
Mr. William Edwards will be thankfully received by The
Bulletin.
Sydney Articled Clerks’ Association has decided that
the recent visit of Sara B. did tend to elevate the drama.
“Nous vous aimerons toujours ,” shouted a Frenchman
from the crowd, as Sara B. steamed off from Sydney
wharf. Which recalls the fact there is in Sydney a
lawyer, now somewhat battered by years and adventures
in the fields of love, w’ho long ago visited Europe, and
came back knowing only one French phrase: “Jevom
aimerai toujours.''
The new Gaiety burlesque, Melbourne Princess’,
“ Cinder Ellen,” is mainly from Fred Leslie’s pen, and he
enacts a servant, something like the Lone Fisherman, in-
terfering with all the business, though with plenty of
singing and dancing. Miss Farren is Cinder Ellen, the
two sisters being Misses Grey and Levey, while Misses
Pedley and Blenheim are two equerries. Danby will
have a better chance as a funny kind of Ally Sloper, Sir
Ludgate Hill, father of the family, the Baron Balderdash
of Byron’s “ Cinderella.” Fred. Storey is Prince Bel-
gravia. The four biggest Melbourne firms have worked
on the costumes. Designs by Wilhelm, of London, and
Hugh Paterson, of Melbourne. Goatcher’s scenery.
Leopold Jordan, late of Sydney D.T., is now in the
States and does well managing theatrical companies on
tour. He has written a play, “Dorothy’s Dilemma,”
which is to be produced by the clever Rose Coghlan.
“ That it possesses a military interest is attested,” says a
Bulletin correspondent, “by the posters, which depict
the buxom Rose, arrayed in Hussar uniform, seated on a
fiery steed which cavorts about the stage. Miss Coghlan
is not yet very certain about her equitation, and maybe
seen cantering nervously round Central Park under the
tutelage of Leopold aforesaid. There is a tremendous
fad for stage realism here. What they chiefly approve is
homely rustic comedy of the blameless order; public
appreciation whereof, amounting to delirium, may be
ensured by the introduction of a real pigstye containing
a real pig fed with real kitchen refuse emitting a real stale
odour and attracting real flies.”
Copy of letter received by Photographer Falk from
Madame Bernhardt
My dear Falk, —I thank you a thousand and thousand
times for the adorable and artistic photographs which you
have made of me ; they are the prettiest and most faith-
ful. I take both your hands and tell you once more
thanks and au-revoir.— Sarah Bernhardt.
Half-a-dozen of the above-mentioned photos, lie before
us. They are undoubtedly fine and very faithful pictures
and much superior to those taken in Melbourne by this
artist; nevertheless, “ Falk” is not so gushing over them
as is his lovely sitter, one of the worst w’ho ever posed,
says he—never still a moment, and impatient of being
placed in suitable postures.

There has been a slaughter among the balloonists

Some humorous amateur at Maryborough (Q.) recently'
sang into the phonograph that pathetic song, “ You’ll
Remember Me.” Some other humorous individual repro-
duced it at Rockhampton as having been warbled by Sims
Reeves, and the people rushed to hear it in consequence.
And when the melancholy instrument had croaked outlets
melody, about six people were moved to tears by a flood
of old, sad recollections, and went away saying that the
sound of that never-to-be-forgotten voice awoke joyous
memories of their childhood’s days, and that they would
have recognised it among a thousand. So say s a Maryr-
borough paper—only it doesn’t say it as nicely as we do.
Edward Solomon, the composer, is described as a man
who can set furniture-polish or a new tonic to music.
He made his first start in life when he travelled with Fred.
Maccabe, and made his first hit when he composed the
music of “ Billee Taylor.” He is as popular with women
as they are with him, and has acted up to the historic
name he bears.

Snazelle, it is averred, is embarking on a literary
career.
An American actress was asked, the other day, whether
she ever rehearsed stage-kisses. “Not repeatedly', but
only just once. I don’t care to be surprised by a peculiar
sort of kiss on the night of a performance, so I alway s
have an actor show me beforehand just how he is going to
do it. There’s nothing in acting that I dread so much as
a kiss. No, I’m not a prude, and do not suffer from the
actual contact of a polite and good-looking fellow’s lips,
but there are more chances of disaster in a stage-kiss than
in almost any action in the drama. In the first place, it
is apt to disturb my make-up. Then, the actor may be a
nervous man and pull away at my wig, if I’m wearing one,
or he may clutch me so tight that when he lets me go I
lose my balance and get laughed at.”

A train loaded with orchestras and choral societies has
fallen through a viaduct in Switzerland, and 60 individuals
will never sing again. They disappeared in the darkness,
and the river Birs is full C« tenors and sopranos, and the
authorities are still fishing baritones out of the turbid
current, and dead basso profundos are hitched among the
snags. Also 150 damaged trombonists, bassoonists, horn-
blowers, and such are in the hospital, and whole choirs
had to be taken away with their legs broken ; and every
charitable institution in Bale is crowded up with contused
Eisteddfodds, and insensible Philharmonic societies, and
stunned choruses, and wreckage, and organ-grinders.

POINTS
The greasiest politician often climbs to
die top of the poll.
In Kaweah, Gronlund’s Commonwealth,
men and women receive equal wages.
Hansard should be the Bible of the
People as well as of the Parliamentarians.
According to Tom Glassey, there are in
Queensland 130,000 adult males, of whom 40,000
are not voters.
The M‘Carthyites aver that Parnell’s ad-
herents after the general election will not number
more than five.
Sydney D.T. credits Kidd, of Camden,
with a speech of Jawbone Neild’s. Both gentle-
men feel annoyed.
Lessing : “It is bad when a king is a vul-
ture amid the carcasses, and worse when he is a
carcass amid the vultures.” In which class is the
coming K. ?
Andy Kelly, M.P. : “ The man who won’t
tike the trouble to register his vote, shouldn’t
have one, and I’d make registration and voting
compulsory.”
Sir Archibald Michie was the author of
the stinging phrase, “the wealthy lower orders,”
and not Chief J ustice Higinbotham, as asserted
in a Sydney journal.
South Australia receives nearly £70,000
a year by her halfpenny land-tax on unimproved
values. It is likely that another halfpenny will
be put on before long.
“ William Trenwith, Esq., M L.A.,” was
announced to lecture in Melbourne last week.
“ Esq.” is bad. It is the first rung of the ladder
on the descent to “Sir.”
In Parkes’ article in the Contemporary
Review for July, he describes his opponents as
“ men of useless lives—the most worthless part of
the Australian population.”
For the Calcutta native press to declare,
as it does, that England governs India by brute
force is as wicked as it would be to say that
England imposed opium on China by brute force.
B. R. Wise now talks very advanced de-
mocracy. Had he been as outspoken ere the tide
turned, he could to-day have been the coining
N.S.W. Premier, with 50 Labour-members at his
back.
According to the British Chancellor, “the
bare statement of a sovereign is equivalent to the
word of a subject under oath.” Very likely it is.
The bare statement of anybody is about equal to
his oath.
Parkes had better blow his political candle
out suddenly and not let it peter out slowly
leaving a bad smell. Een his erawlsomest
friends admit that he is beginning to drivel
shockingly.
The N.S.W. Legislative Assembly contains
141 members, and only one out of the lot dares
openly to say a single word in favour of plural
voting. There is a fact for the English newspapers
to chew over.
It is said that the N.S.W. squatters,
having nobbled the irrigation department in a
roundabout way, are now trying to engineer the
Forestry branch of Lands to their own purposes—
especially in the matter of reserves.
Parkes reluctantly made Bruce Smith
N.S.W. Treasurer. Eager to secure a counterfoil to
his powerful, ambitious colleague, he dangled the
portfolio before Garvan for a whole fortnight;
but that astute, over-solemn Oppositionist only
smelt at the bait.
Balfour declares that he will never consent
to the Irish police being placed under the control
of local bodies Balfour’s game is the direct
opposite—to place the local bodies under the con-
trol of the police. How absurd these things
appear in Australia!
Mr. Ringnose Wise’s lecture, “ What
Parliament can do for Labour,” is interpreted,
perhaps rightly, “What Labour can do for Mr.
B. Ringnose Wise.” His attitude in N.S.W.
Parliament is not pro-Parkes, but pro-Wise, with
a firm grip on the rail, pending the cat’s jump.
Prof. Bryce, writing to Premier Parkes,
joins earnestly in the hope expressed by the latter
that “the peoples of the colonies may decide as
those of the American States did in 1787-90.”
Isn’t this tantamount to trusting that they’ll
make waste paper of the present Commonwealth
Bill ?
According to Adelaide Quiz, the first
known user of the phrase, “One man'one vote,”
was Major Cartwright, who, at the beginning of
the century, wrote ;—“ Every one man sha 1 l have
one vote, and for any man, however rich, to
have any more than one vote is against natural
justice.”
Attorney-General Homburg’s (S. A.) Agri-
cultural Holdings Bill provides for tenants having
a right to all improvements they may make; also
the right to assign tenancy and acquire the tee-
simple of their holdings. If passed by the
Assembly, this radical Bill will be shelved by the
Upper House.
Munro’s recent admission that he had per-
sonally lost £120,000 in one land-company, but
nevertheless held on, is evidence in favour of
having at the head of affairs professional politicians
who really concentrate. Is the fag-end of tho
mind of a man like Munro really good enough to
govern Victoria ?
One rock ahead of the Labour Electoral
Leagues is the growing ascendancy of the Trades
and Labour Councils upon whom it should be
impressed that they represent after all but a
section of the great voting body, and that the
undue use of the Councils’ well-organised influence
may lead to the institution of Australian Tam-
many Halls,
A glorious row took place the other night
in the Parliamentary refreshment-room at Wel-
lington (Maoriland). One of the Northern mem-
bers was using strong language, and a Southern
member gambolled on his prostrate form with both
feet. No names are given, but if the man on top
wasn’t Henry Smith Fish, we will feel more sur-
prised than usual.
Premier Munro has confessed to the Vic-
torian Assembly that Prime Minister Parkes was
the godfather of the term “Commonwealth ” in'
the secret committee of the Federal Convention,
The Bulletin is prepared to bet that Munro is
wrong. Deakin was the originator, and, wishing
to have the term adopted, artfully handed the
idea over to Parkes.
The Bulletin conceives that it is the
duty of the N.S.W. Labour-party to politically
support Parkes simply as the hangman supports
his victim—until the time comes to drop him
through the trap with a dull, sickening thud. If
the Labour-party begin to cuddle Parkes instead
of simply holding him up he will take them down
the trap with him.

Sydney Echo wants Traill to define the
nice distinction he draws between “ intelligence ”
and “intellect.” As Traill mayn’t have the
necessary time on his hands, The Bulletin illus-
trates the difference : Running the Echo requires
intelligence, but not intellect. Thus, we speak of
the “intelligence” of a blackfellow, but of tha
“ intellect ” of Dan O'Connor.
“Socialism means, or wishes to mean,
industrial co-operation, community of interest,
universal sympathy, a paying not more to hand
than head, a greater share than hitherto of the
wealth which can only be produced by the com-
bined efforts of both, and has within it the secret
of an orderly and benign reconstruction of
society.”—J. Russell Lowell.
The S. A. Labour-party are already
organising for the general elections, and in case of
vacancies or a dissolution, they ai’e even now pre-
pared. Out of the 54 seats, they reckon they
could secure at least 20 for bona-fide labour-
members, It was principally the fear of a disso-
lution that induced tho S. A. Assembly to keep
Playford in power a few weeks back.
In the last two N.S.W. Parliaments a Bill
has been put through the Assembly to extend the
Employers’ Liability Act to sea-faring men ; but
no one has ever been found wi'ling to take charge
of the Bill in the Council. For the sake of this
democratic measure alone Parkes should be
“squeezed ” into summoning a democrat (say Mr.
Luscombe) to the Council to advocate the cause of
the sailor at the feet of capitalism.
Both Dibbs and Garvan—the leading Op-
positionists—in N.S.W. Assembly, were disfran-
chised at the late election for East Sydney,
although Dibbs has practically lived at the
Reform Club for 10 years, while Garvan has been
associated with a house in Castlereagh-street
since his infancy—42 years ago. Yet a man who has
not set foot inside the Australian Club for 25
years is on the roll in respect of an alleged room
there.
A Mr. Wickham, who has for over 20 years
held the position of accountant in the N.S.W. rail-
ways, was some time since superseded by a Mr.
Hall, whose principal distinctive qualification
appears to be that he has just arrived from Eng-
land, doncherknow. Mr. Wickham is still
retained, however, at the same salary (£700), and
the new accountant receives £lOOO. So the bleed-
ing country now pays £l7OO for work that pre-
viously cost £7OO.
Cologne Gazette said last week that the
Pope desired war, so that he might regain his
temporalities. How could the Pope be bene-
fited by a war ? Is Atheistical France to give
him his temporalities ? Or Italy ? And what
would either of them gain ? At the same time it
must be said that the Pope is stronger without
any temporalities. The more you take away from
him, the more powerful he is —and that, if they
only knew it, is the case with all clergymen.
That dear, good man, Robert Harper,
explains that the Victorian National Association
is not to be “what is known as ‘ Conservative,’
nor sectional, nor anything except what is free,
liberal, enlightened, and all that sort of thing.
But the gentle lamb further explains that “there
is an element of impending danger in the present
course of politics which furnishes a sufficient
I’eason for the establishment of this Association.”
In short, it is intended as a free, broad, advanced,
unsectional national movement for stoushing the
Labour candidate.
“It is a mistake to think that no section
of men with a political object in view can do
without the aid of one of the great parties. The
Socialist cannot accept the aid of either party.
His plan is not to win the alliance of the Liberals
and the Tories, but to compass their confusion.
He will never make friends of the rent and interest
mongers. His one aim, his one hope, his highest
desire is to destroy them. This is plain English ;
but it is as well we should understand each other.”
The N.S.W. Cabinet are borrowing money
from any institution, person or thing that will
lend. A year ago M‘MiHan had £3,000,000 of
loan-money in his possession ; within the last six
months he borrowed £1,785,000 in London and
Sydney, borrowing as low a sum as £12,000 in one
instance; and in addition to these moneys the
Treasury absorbed £4,000,000 worth of trust-
funds. When he left office a few weeks ago, he
had spent £9,000,000 for which no provision had
been made.
Two things are now plain to The Bul-
letin : (1) that to prevent personation there
must be electoral registration, and (2) that
registration must, in the democratic inter-
est, be made compulsory, just as the registra-
tion of births, deaths, and marriages is compul-
sory. The poor man who hustles for his living
will, unless legally compelled to register, so often
neglect to get his ticket that his disfranchisement
through default will more than counterbalance
the effect of the abolition of plural-voting.
Editor Higgs still noisily uses the few
opportunities left him for venting personal ill-
humour in the Australian Workman by slating
those legislators who have sufficient vigour and
ability to earn money honestly outside of legisla-
tive work, and denying that they may fairly earn
anything beyond their Parliamentary pay. Would
Mr. Higgs, a partner in a (no doubt remunera-
tive) printing business, have been in a position of
sufficient independence to write thus had he,
instead of suffering defeat, been returned foi
South Sydney ? Hardly !
The N.S.W. Government organ is shying
big pellets at Parkes’ Some-Men-One-Yote Bill-
under the friendly cover of a cynical contributor.
The Bill has been proved to be a delusion and a
snare, as far as democracy is concerned. And yet
the Sydney D.T., built up with the pennies of the
workers, will not itself openly and directly blast
the abortion. Had such a disgraceful measure
emanated from Dibbs or Traill, the province
would have been warned in hysterical fashion to
beware of a measure as remarkable for its clumsy
machinery as for its treachery to democratic
principles.
Dear Bulletin, —The ranks of the Labor-
party in N.S.W. Parliament include at least one
traitor. The sooner this skunk is drummed out
of the caucus-room the better for the cause of
democracy. Not a caucus of this new political
power is held but a synopsis of its deliberations
is conveyed straight to Bruce Smith. One in-
stance alone need be quoted to prove the above
statement. Recently the Labour-party—all the
merinos—passed an important resolution relevant
to a measure before the Assembly. The caucus
had scarcely been risen 10 minutes, when a
straight and true Labour-member actually saw a
verbatim et litteratim copy of the resolution in
Bruce Smith’s hands. The Parkes Cabinet are
accurately and promptly posted up in all the
doings and sayings of the Labour-party.—S.S.B.
Says the Australasian, dealing with the
N.S.W. Electoral Reform Bill
As a matter of course, the “ One-Man-One-Yote ” clause
is made an essential part, and the spendthrifts will have
equal power with the thrifty workers, and the illiterate
and criminals will be considered to have as much know-
ledge of political affairs as the educated classes and those
who scrupulously obey the law. The tneory seems to be
that the voting power of the elector should be in inverse
ratio to his ability.
This is begging the question with a vengeance.
The slavish organ of Property starts off with the
assumption that the wealthy are invariably
thrifty, well-educated, intelligent, and well-
behaved, and that , the propertyless are inevitably
spendthrifts, criminals, illiterates, and ignora-
muses. Of course this is not true ; even if it were,
it furnishes the strongest of arguments for the
alteration of a social system that, by unequal
division of the world’s goods, unequally divides,
also, the world’s goodness.
Dear Bulletin,— Parkes is playing a wily
game with the N.S.W. Labour-party. Unless
they show more nous and spout less often, the old
Parliamentary hand will dish them body and soul.
His Electoral Bill is craftily framed : One-Man-
One-Vote is to be enacted, if Parkes is allowed to
have his sweet will, in one Bill, but is to be con-
trolled in its use by another Bill, constituting the
electorates, which may be contracted or expanded
to suit the waning fortunes of a Cabinet. The
Representation of the People Bill does not stand
on its own bottom, but is readable with the Seats
Redistribution Bill, which has not yet seen the
light of day. Parkes wishes to push the former
Bill through the Assembly, before taking up tho
second Bill. His game, of course, is to get the
One-Man-One-Vote Bill out of the hands of the
Assembly, and then tell that House it is corn-
raited to the Seats Arranging Bill, and therefore
is bound in honour to pass it. Let the Labour-
party examine all Parkes’ political pills.—W. ,T.
Those who argue that the attack made in
N.S.W. Parliament on Judge Docker’s manner of
administering what he calls justice was cowardly
because the attacked “had no opportunity to
reply,” should remember that it was Judge
Docker’s persistent unnecessary attacks on
prisoners, witnesses, and jurors who really had no
opportunity of reply that provoked members’
criticism. Besides, it is absurd to say that a
J udge is defenceless. He can write to the papers
(as many Judges have done), and even the meanest
Judges are religiously toadied to by the daily
press, which has in N.S.W. unblushingly com-
pounded many absolute judicial crimes. The
J udges have to be taught (and the day approaches
when they will be taught) that there is nothing
“ sacred ” about their office any more than there
is about that of the scavenger—that they are
simply the overpaid servants of a long suffering
community, and that if they are cheeky to their
masters they will be fired right out just as a grocer
fires out his rebellious shopboy. The first thing to
be done to bring them to their senses is to strip
them and their minions of their absurd wigs and
millinery.
Three times last week did Premier Parkes
object, in N.S.W. Parliament, to certain things
being done, not because such things were in them-
selves bad or illogical, but because they weren’t
dope in England. “What would be thought in
Hingland, if,”&c.,&c. This miserable dependence
on the precedents of a country foreign to most of
our population recalls a characteristic incident in
tho political life of Peter Lalor. During his
tenure of the Speakership of the Victorian
Assembly he had a controversy—one of a multi-
tude —with David Gaunson. The question at
issue was one concerningParliaraentary procedure,
and Gaunson quoted Sir Erskine May in support
of his views. The Speaker differed from the hon.
member, and was not to be swayed by the opinion
of May. “ But,” said Gaunson, “he is the greatest
authority on Parliamentary procedure.’’ “ Who
says so?” mildly asked Peter. “Ho is so con-
sidered in the English Parliament,” replied the-
other. “He may be good enough for the English,
Parliament,” announced the Speaker, “ but he is
of no authority here.” “Then who is the
authority here ? ” persisted Gaunson. “lAM I”
exclaimed Lalor in a voice of thunder. Australia,
wants men who dare to make precedents.

New South Wales’s new Treasurer is more
wealthy than her Treasury.

accommodation on the Mariposa. .
Mr.; (afterwards Chief-Justice) Martin was
once debarred from entering theN.S.W. Assembly
by a money qualification.
One of the Melbourne knights is con-
tinually in hot water over the troubled companies,
and another is buying them up.
David Syme has deserted the Colonial
Bank, and transferred the colossal Age account to
premier Monro’s Federal Bank.
Bruce Smith is the third barrister Avho
has filled the Colonial Treasurership. W. A.
Long and H. E. Cohen were the others.
Madame Blavatsky’s stimulants were black
coffee and cigarettes. Her editorial associate,
Mrs. Besant, sticks to bread and milk.
There are 160 total-abstainers in the
British House of Commons. That is exclusive of
those lately expelled for immoral practices.
They are to have an exhibition in London
illustrating the Victorian age. With Albert
Edward’s gambling-kit on show, success is
assured.
E. J. Clark, sub-editor of S.A. Register,
has succeeded the late David Bews (late S.A.
Minister of Education) as editor and part-proprie-
tor of Wallaroo Times.

previqusly. From this we gather that while
pimping the broomstick may be nice, signing the
Registrar’s book is perfect bliss.

The late O’Gorman Mahon once rose and
informed the House of Commons that there wore
only three personages who had beyond all doubt a
title to the “The,” and they were The Pope,
The Devil, and The O’Gorman Mahon.
Bishop Camidge (C. of E., Bathurst), when
an undergraduate at Oxford in 1860, laid the top
stone on the spire of Wakefield parish church. He
climbed the dizzy height by ladders placed on the
outside of the spire, and, having hud the stone,
then stood upon it—at a height of 240 feet.

Britain and Ireland and other Regions, Illustrious
Empress of India.” Is the Pope a democrat ?

The clover Mr, Alfred Bennett, of Sydney
E. News, is much more touchy than the much
less brilliant Mr. David Syme, of Melbourne
Age. When the Australian Star applied the
term Ananias to the E. News Alfred brought
suit for libel; but the Age for 35 years has (jour-
nalistically) staggered beneath the nickname and
yet come out on top.
The late Professor Wilson, of Melbourne
University, was a slave to duty. When his class
would take a holiday for a football or cricket-
match, the Professor would attend the class-room
and deliver his lecture to empty benches. The
most inconvenient result, to the students, was
that the items in that particular lecture were sure
to be embodied in the exam.-papers.
Durant Yernon Harcourt died in Mel-
bourne last week. He was an attorney, but
intimately mixed up in journalism, and well
known in social coteries, such as the old-time
Bohemians, and the Owls of a more recent day.
He had a hand, with Capt. Henry Morin
Humphreys, in the Magpie, over which the Cap-
tain dropped his £l2OO ; Marcus Clarke’s Humbug
was of the same era and kidney.

" Rev. Arthur F. Collier, a Sydney clergy-
man, will probably make a big fortune out of his
patent “audible telephone,” which speaks with a
loud voice (over 5000 miles, it is averred) and will
do away with the present necessity of rising from
one’s desk to listen to a message when the instru-
ment rings up. This telephone will enable every
well-regulated clergyman to hear the faintest
message from Divine Providence calling him to_a
billet with a higher screw.
Sir Redmond Barry was very pedantic as
to his tailoring. No other Melbourne man dressed
as he did. Always wearing very tight pantaloons
tightly strapped under foot, he looked more bke a
military officer in mufti than a Supreme Court
Judge. He once gave the name of his tailor to a
well-known “Block ” man, who instantly ordered
trousers on the Barry model. This gentleman,
having inserted himself into the garments, found,
on sitting down, that they split completely. On
complaining to the tailor, he was consoled with
the information that as he did not state which
description of the Barry pantaloons he wanted, he
had been accommodated with a pair of Sir Red-
mond’s “ walking ” trousers.
The London & Sydney Tea Co. supply families,
and deliver teas free anywhere in N.S. Wales.
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla is too well known to require
the aid of any exaggerated or fictitious certificate.
Witnesses of the marvellous'cures effected by this
preparation are to-day living in every country of
the globe.
The Land of the Crocodile.
(BY MAROOGALINE.)
The nose of Queensland’s Colonial Secre-
tary, Horace Tozer, has been a “prominent
feature ” in a recent Gympio press cor-
respondence. The true story of that nose shall
now be related. Tozer’s Homan proboscis was
aggravated by occasional visits of eczema, and
Jack Hamilton, M.L.A., recommended lubri-
cation with a weak solution of salicylic acid.
Now, when Kelland, the Gympie democrat,
looked in at the railway window and briefly
remarked “Bco-hoo !” Tozer was in the attitude
of a Hielanman regaling himself with a pinch of
snuff, the thumb used for applying Hamilton’s
lubricant, and the rest of the hand extended at
the usual angle. Kelland somewhat naturally
assumed that this was a gesture of derision, in-
tended as an affront to the local democracy.
Archelaus, the ancient philosopher, held to the
theory that goats breathe through their ears;
Kelland is of opinion that Tozer conducts satiric
dialogues with his nose. Alas! as old Gariy e
observed, “All war is a misunderstanding!
Tozer is not a man capable of an act of vulgarity
either to an individual or a crowd. I have met
him in joy and sorrow. Last week we were
unusually seasick together on a private open-sea
fishing-excursion. Tozer slipped on a piece of
bait and sat down unexpectedly on the deck, after
throwing up a new Mining Bill, while Morgan,
the Chairman of Committees, unwillingly
accepted several violent notices of motion.
# * *
No reporter has yet discovered that Sir
Thomas MTlwraith had a recent narrow escape.
The “Knight of Auchenflower” was down at
Stradbroke Island in the steamer Miner, and
about to leave on the return journey. The Miner
was anchored off the shore, and Thomas started
out to her in a small boat rowed by one of the
crew. A heavy wave capsized-the dingy, and
the Colonial Treasurer, the boatman, and a big
dog were at once wrong end to the zenith. With
a violent effort the boatman'pushed Sir Thomas
into shallow water, with a hazy idea that he was a
strand eel whale (weight 18 stone), and the first
question the noble knight asked was “Where is
the dog?” That faithful collie had mysteriously
disappeared. The boatman swam out, turned
over the boat, and the collie jumped out and swam
ashore as if nothing had happened ! He had begin
safe in the air imprisoned beneath. About 35
years ago a schooner was washed ashore on the
beach south of the Tweed Heads, and old Tom
Boyd and a mate cut a hole in the bottom and
rescued two French sailors, one of whom is still
alive. Sir Thomas swimming ashore with his
Budget speech reminds me of Csesar with his
‘‘Commentaries’’and'Camcens with the “Lusiad.”
Dr. Nisbet, of Townsville, is a son of
W. D. Nisbet. so long and favourably known as
Queensland’s Engineer of Harbours and Rivers.
When this festive Esculapian was a boy he was a
regular patron of circuses, concerts, and other
attractive frivolities where his enthusiasm usually
expressed itself in wild applause with hands and
feet. Once he was sent into the Highlands to
stay with an esteemed old grandmother of the
severest Puritanic school.' He accompanied this
reverend old dame to church, and when the choir
had finished singing “ Old Hundred ” young
Nisbet broke out violently and unexpectedly in a
demonstrative encore ! His grandmother didn’t
live long after that night.
The gaol-regulations at Thursday Island
are based on the moat advanced principles of
humanity. Some of the prisoners stroll about the
island, catch fish, collect botanical specimens, go
to sleep or get drunk with beautiful impartiality.
Yet nobody ever escapes, and no harm is e.ver
done. If you meet anyone there out for a walk,
be careful to ascertain if he is a boarder from the
gaol or a wealthy pearl"fisher.'' Thus the gaoler to
Tommy Abdalla; “Look here, Tommy, if you
are not in by 10 o’clock I’ll lock you out ! ” “ All
right, Massa,” said Tommy ; “ suppose I no drunk,
I come home all right ! ” Some one owed Tommy
a sum of £7 for over a year, and, seeing no pros-
pect of payment, Tommy forged a cheque for £l4
in the name of the debtor. Tommy’s defence was :
“ One year this man he owe me £7 ; he no pay me.
Now, I owe him £7, and no pay him for one year
more ! ” The sublime humour of this financial
transaction was wasted on the local J.P., who sent
Tommy into retirement for six months, but,
during that time, he was so disgusted with the
accommodation of the local gaol that when be
came out he went away indignantly to try <|he
Croydon Bastille, and is probably there at the
present time.
# * *
When Colonel Olcott was in Brisbane he
spent a Sunday with me a few miles out of town.
At his special request I gave him an exhibition of
boomerang-throwing, the first he had ever seen,
and he was greatly pleased. Personally he is a
very genial old gentleman and his persuasiveness
is testified to eloquently by the fact that he in-
duced me to write a long article for the Indian
Theosophist on the “ Habits and Superstitions of
the Queensland Blacks.” My reward for that
article is to be hereafter, or even later on, when
“ In India’s realms Elysian ” the genial Colonel
and I are instructing the Mahatmas and the
avenging spirits who are to wipe out the capital-
ists, the Mammon worshippers, the money-lenders,
the fat landlords, the titled loafers and sundry
other unspeakable human abominations who
encumber that beautiful planet we once in-
habited.

Many a Bulletin reader will learn with
some astonishment that a clergyman who distin-
guished himself in Brisbane in the manipulation
of title-deeds and various financial eccentricities
among confiding parishioners, is now a curate in a
N. S. Wales parish. His name is not in the
Church list, so his bishop, with true modern
Christian consideration, is “keeping him dark”
until, like the virtuous French Louis, he has
“made the amende honorable to God,” and lijved
down the misunderstanding with his Queensland
congregation.

A Big Renunciation.
Prince Albert of Monaco, the proprietor of the
gambling casino at Monte Carlo, has, according
to the cable, renounced £50,000 a year rent and
privilege payments, and is about to convert the
building into a free consumptive hospital. Neerb
less to say the unselfish decision was not all of his
own making. A man rarely rises to such a charity
as this without the advocacy of the step by a
woman he loves and respects, and in this case the.
princess was the dominant factor. £50,000 a year
is an enormous sum and a great power to give
up for the sake of what the good-humoured will
term a chimera, and the envious a fad. It is in
round numbers £250 a day. It represents all that
luxury*could suggest or money buy but life; it
could silence any but the voice of the Last Col-
lector, calling for the payment of his inevitable,
bill, it could-buy off every person orrthing but
death. And so it is a big renunciation, and is
only transcended in the mind; of some, men by
the renunciation of lifb itself. No matter how
fortified one may be with the knowledge of a
happier world, wlure pain is unknown, and from
whence death is for ever banished, the giving up
of friends, parents, sister, brother, husband, wife;
the conviction that the earthly sun is ours for so
short a time, the horror that this, our beloved
body, the dear familiar house of the soul that is a
stranger to us, must go to its matrix before
its time, is a greater pain, and carries a greater
sacrifice than any other in this * world. Even
if prostrated by foreign disease—that is disease
which is eradicable, and there are only one or two
which are not —the memory looks backward to that
dear time when wo had health, and seemed only
made for love and the employment of the sun,
when the golden beams filtered through the sap
green tinted leaves, and we felt that we were part
of that perpetual summer and necessary to it.
The seasons come and go, but we come no more,
and by and bye others who have taken our
prematurely emptied places follow us also before
their time. The most fruitful source of. death is
kidney disease. If the kidneys are kept in sound
working order the blood is maintained in a pure
state, and this being done at least ninety percent,
of diseases, not excepting epidemics, are safely
avoided. Warner’s safe„cure and pills is the only
absolute curative (so far discovered) for this and
kindred disorders. Hundreds of persons with
their feet tottering on the brink of the grave have
been snatched from early death just in time by the
use of the safe remedies, and men of over three
scdrft years, Vvhoke systems were rapidly.breaking
up under the venom of uric acid poison,- have been
restored to sound .health, and many years of
happy useful lives. Warner’s remedies are
absolutely destructive to kidney and liver disease,
and the fact has been proved times out of number.

who asks literary questions in Parliament. The
Bulletin doesn’t wonder. Parkes has doled out
some good things to people in the office where the
Echo is printed—in fact, judging from the way
his patronage has been exercised, you’d imagine
that the whole literary ability of N.S. Wales was
centred in the Herald office—which is, from the
very nature of it, the last place under Heaven to
look for anybody who is really original and clever.
Mr. Charley Lyne, Parkes’ particular pal (under-
stood to be at the present moment fixing up the old
man’s autobiography), was appointed secretary of
the N.S.W. Public Works’ Committee at £7OO a
year—a salary which has since been added to ;
another Herald man left the Fairfaxes’ service
lately to draw £l2OO a year for compiling a dry-as-
dust history of N.S. Wales from the ancient
records ; yet another Herald man recently
dropped into a Parkesian-Government billet at
£450 a year ; yet another fell on his feet at the
feet of Parkes to the tune of £400; still another
cx-Herald scribe is drawing £950 annually with
more to follow ; only the other day the dear, good
old Premier gave a man £2OO (or guineas) from
the Treasury for doing literary work which lots
of men could do, and do well, inside of four days.
What price the vaunted impartiality of the Press
under such circumstances ?
* # *
Some modest individual who nas borrowed
not the name but only the initials of that clever
and popular artist organist Neville G. Barnet—
or who takes a mean advantage of a coincidence
in initials—writes a long and violent criticism in
Sydney Evening News upon the performances of
City Organist Wiegand. Anybody who knows
Organist Barnet will appreciate the greatness of
this literary error. For the unreasoning public
suffers from an impression that a literary critic
is always a broken-down writer, and that an
art critic is a decayed artist who gave up canvas
and paint because his horses generally had toes,
and his figures had two left legs and no right one;
and in the same way it may think that an organ-
critic is a soured organ-blowtr. And as, in the
present case, they will naturally think that
organist Neville Barnet is the critic in question
—which, to any right-minded person aware
of the cordial relations which always subsist
between truly great professional musicians, is
manifestly impossible —it is all the more
essential that Mr. Barnet should expose the
mistake at once. The News’ writer demands of
the man of European fame, whose organ-playing
delighted the musical centres of civilisation,
“educative” and “progressive” music, also
“pure and reverent treatment,” whatever that
may be, and “ true purity of style,’’ whatever that
may be also, and “ purity of interpretation ” like-
wise, whatever that may be, and when one man
drivels like that over another man’s initials, the
case requires prompt attention. If Mr. Barnet
fails to suppress this other man, people will pre-
sently be suggesting that, after organist Wiegand
playing the kist of whistles and organist Barnet
—for the common herd don’t know any better
writing to the News, fair play demands that
Barnet should play the instrument just once, while
Wiegand writes to the News, and this would
necessarily lead to much ill-feeling. Moreover,
the writer—whoever it is—finishes up by stating
that if Wiegand doesn’t conduct himself properly,
the Evening News will “insist on the necessity”
of something or other, and the sight of that state-
ment, unjustifiably placed above anybody’s initials,
is enough to rouse to frenzy any genuine and
generous artist like Mr. Neville G. Barnet.
* # •
The News seems to be annoyed particu-
larly just here because Wiegand doesn’t always
grind out old, pompous, dryasdust classical
rubbish, in an empty ball, but rather plays
something with a tune in it that the people
can understand, and gets all Sydney for
his audience, and the light and gladsome
character of Mr. Barnet gives the lie to this
theory right off. The people pay for the organ,
and pay to hear the organ, and if they want
“ Yankee Doodle ” on the organ they are going to
have it. Mr. Barnet knows this, and, we are
sure, feels glad in consequence; it is only the man
with the similar initials who can’t see it.

A leading Australian military authority
recently designed a number of buildings—kitchens,
caretaker s house, &c. —in connection with a cer-
tain defence-work, and, while locating fireplaces,
made no provision for chimnies. ’Twas the_ same
officer who recently, on inspecting a section of
local defence force, found fault with the appear-
ance of the men on parade. “Why,” he asked
the commanding officer, “ Why—aw —do you not
—aw —make youah men weah the same —aw the
same kind of boots —aw —and twim theah beawds
alike—aw ?”

* * *
Things have reached such a pitch in some of
the big Melbourne unlicensed coffee-temples that
boarders now openly bring grog to table. At one
timeliq nor-drinking in these godly places was especi-
allyprohibited by the managements, though almost
every lodger had a trunk-full of whisky in his
bedroom. Under present circumstances, wherein
consists the special virtue of residing in unlicensed
houses? The competition of the coffee-palaces
has so greatly improved hotel-accommodation tin t
the unlicensed “palaces” are being completely
knocked out. It was all very well to be teetotally
virtuous while such virtue paid 10 per cent,
dividends.

The Bulletin reads with a cynical leer
the Australian and English newspaper-offers made
from time to time, of prizes for stories and
pictures. Most of these are made—according to
the superhuman gall of the man who draws up
the advertisement —for the purpose of “encourage-
ing local talent,” and unearthing some; mute,
inglorious Dickens or Titian who has hitherto
blushed unseen in Bungaree. At least that is
what he says ; what he means, if we may judge
from results, is just this ;
PRIZE COMPETITION.
The owners of the have resolved to offer a
prize of £5O for an Easter tale, and trust that they may
thus turn to profitable account such of their literary
fellow-creatures as are not gifted with arithmetic. They
calculate on receiving, at the lowest estimate, say, 400
good tales, equal to about 2000 columns of reading-
matter, and they will publish pretty nearly the whole lot.
This mass of literature would have cost, say, £lOOO, even
at low rates, hut owing to the lavish generosity of this
paper it saves £950, and gets its name up at the same tune.
It is expressly stipulated that the paper doesn’t pay one
solitary cent for any save the prize tale. In delivering
the prize to the successful competitor the paper will wear
an aspect of wild and boundless munificence, which will
make people think it is giving itself away as a burnt
offering on the altar of literature. Further, there will be
sundry pathetic observations about the weary editor, and
how he has had to toil through a dreary waste of MSS.
He would have had to toil just the same if there hadn't
been any “competition,” for,the old publication has got to
lie filled up every week; but these observations are
heartrending, and arouse commiseration. Full particulars
as to conditions, &c., will be found below.

An overdressed woman, loaded with
jewellery, the other day visited ; the cookery-
classes at Fort-street (Sydney) State school, and
was apparently much pleased at the culinary
accomplishments of the girls. “Ah,” said she,
with a haughty and comprehensive hand-wave,
indicating the cookery-pupils, “we shall soon ’ave
a lot o’ good servants about.” “Excuse me,
madam,” remarked the lady-principal—“ these are
being made into good mistresses.” That is the
great Australian want—good mistresses.
# * *
The N.S.W. Prevention of Cruelty to
Children Society have tested the proportions of
the Sydney baby-industry with the result that, out
of 80 applicants for the charge of a suppositious
child, 75 “kind ladies” undertook that it would
“ never be heard of again.” Their fees for a baby.
murder ranged from £3 to £3O, one baby-farmer
demanding £5O for the job. In a Sydney baby-
farm recently, eight helpless children were dis-
covered in a damp cellar in which one could
barely swing a cat round without striking the
walls. That devout political invertebrate, the
N.S.W. Minister for Justice, confesses to an
intimate official knowledge of the baby-farming
business, but he has been too busy to bring the old
hags up with a round turn. In the last 10-years,
62,000 children under the age of five have died in
N.S.W.
* * *
In N.S.W., beyond question, one law
exists for the rich and another for the poor. Ihe
public officials of the province are governed by an
act of Parliament which says that no officer under
the age of 60 shall be retired on a pension, unless
on the ground of some infirmity of health. Last
year the son of an ex-Speake_r of the N.o.W.
Assembly was retired on a pension of £l9O m his
40th year, on the ground, it is said, of “ loss of
memory. 1 ’ So far so good ! But this Herculean-
built gentleman is shortly heard of on ’Change as a
broker; and a month or two later as a candidate
for Parliamentary honours —of course, in the in-
terests of Freetrade and Parkes. The Bulletin
rises to suggest that a man whose memory is good
enough to recollect the virtues of Foreign-trade,
is also good enough to serve the public in his
former station ; and that, if he will not respond
to the call of the Civil Service Board to return to
his billet, the Civil servants should be relieved of
the incubus of supplementing his screw.
# * *
Australian art item, from the London
Art Journal :
In Australia the happy idea has been hit upon by the
president, Mr. E. L. Montefiore, of illustrating, in water-
colours, the scenery of N.S.W., its coasts, the rivers,
lakes and mountains. The Art Gallery have opened a
competition in the matter, and invite drawings of these
subjects, to be submitted to them not later than the end
of next November, and they announce their resolution,
should these he good enough, to purchase a dozen at £o>
each. Unfortunately there is hardly time for an English
artist to enter the lists, otherwise a good one might not
only sweep the board, hut probably make his journey
remunerative by other sales.
Of course he might. A British editor never takes
in the possibility of there being even one decent
Australian artist in existence, nor does be allow
for the fact that good British artists are more or
less scarce at present, and that British art galleries
are considerably filled up by French painters,
to make up for the terrible decadence of the
local article. Also he never ran against
a ° snag in the shape of an Australian
board of judges, which might also disarrange
his calculation. Further he forgets that the
British artist paints everything bright, green,
and when the latter struck this brown and yellow
laud, with its sky of glaring blue, and its sun of
brass, he would probably be a flabbergasted
sinudger, and long ere lie got the hang of
things the competition would be over. Most
likely he would paint Australia green, and then
the board of judges would heave him out.
England is rich with the grass that grows on the
dead man’s grave ; and no Australian can there
look upon the sward without thinking of worms
and corruption, for the land is crowded up, and
underneath every clod some forgotten sepulchre
is helping the vegetation along ; and the artist
who has been trained in that school can’t paint
the drought-stricken plains and the murky bush.
He would only burst if he made the attempt, and
take a steerage passage home, leaving his canvases
and his shabby check clothes in pawn with
Abraham—if Abraham dared to advance anything
upon them. Egotism, thy name is J. Bull!

ON THE SQUARE.
Cheeks with the flush of pear,
Eyes with a rogueish stare,
Lips panting “ if you dare
Surely you’ll rue
Graceful the pace of her,
Winning the grace of her,
Charming the face of her,
Really too-too.
Limbs like a mountain pine,
Arms like a clinging vine.
Hands I would clasp in mine,
Waist I’d ensnare;
Rapture ! the beam of her
Eyes, and the gleam of her
Clieeks, and the dream of her
Tremulous hair !
Teeth behind red lips lie
White as the fleecy sky,
Smile chasing smile and sigh
Over her face;
Heavens! the dip of her
Waist, and tin trip of her
Ueet, and the hip of her
Pen cannot trace !
Tootseys with tapered too.
Ankles, a vision— oh
Sin to he writing so—
Sinful but true:
See how they stare at her.
Eye-glasses bear at her,
Old fogies glare at her,
Young fogies, too.
Womanly, twenty-three,
Maidenly, fancy free,
Who can this angel be,
Young and so fair?
Breathless, I ask, and wise
' Browne, of the bank,replies,
“ Pretty, but, dash my eyes 1
It's on the square .”
Brisbane.

being placed in position in the tower would

* * *
Canon Dendy, who died in Adelaide last
week, was probably one of the most old-fashioned
and eccentric of Australian clergymen. He used
to frequently slate his congregation from the
cathedral pulpit for their habit of putting three-
penny pieces in the hat.

Four books, with Queen Victoria’s auto-
graph, are kept in a glass-case at Melbourne Public
Library. Even the autographs are not shown,
for fear that the pages whereon they are should
be soiled, but the books bear an awesome inscrip-
tion, informing the beholder that the Queen s
autograph is within. Why are not hassocks pro-
videdhat one may comfortably kneel before the
fetish ?

Is it a “ corner ” joke ? We are told that
a smart youngster asked Sir Julian Salomons the
other day; “Please, Sir Julian, is this Phillip-
street?” “Yes, my boy,” kindly answered the
new knight. “ Ah, I was quite sure it was, but I
thought I’d get counsel’s opinion on it.” And ho
soon left a big space between himself and the
dapper little Q.C.

From both houses of the N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, last week, some ladies had to retire in con-
fusion, on account of the unsavory subject under
discussion. Attorney-General Simpson's refer-
ences to the operation of the criminal law made
two fair stenographers beat a hasty retreat from
the Council; while Davis, M.P., unwittingly per-
petrated a double entendre in the Assembly, and
some ladies behind the bar ran away from the
sound of the guffaws it called forth.

Way back in the early S.A. days a black-
fellow playfully sawed a Caucasian’s head off with
a rusty hand-saw. For this offence the simple
sawyer was condemned to be hanged. The gaol-
chaplain attended him to the scaffold and
requested him to engage in prayer. “.Tacky,”
said he, “you bin very bad man, you going to
die, you rnakoe pray.” “Baal,” said the scared
•Tacky, trembling in every limb, “you raakee
pray, me too plurry prightened !”

* # •
Somebody advertises in the Age wanting
an owner for a set of false teeth found in a dredge.
Toothless people will please make a note of this.
Anybody who likes to get teeth out of a dredge
will find this lot to be just the sort of teeth he was
looking for. For our own part we don’t care for
them. We always get our own teeth out of an
ash-cart, and having arrived at a green old age,
we don’t care to change our habits.

‘‘Our Pleasant Yices,” an Australian
story from the bright pen of Milner Mac Master,
the Melbourne Daily Telegraph writer who,
over the pen name of “Viator,” was about the
onlv Melbourne writer of consequence who took a
democratic view of the position and had a good
and humane word for Labour during the late
strike, will shortly be published by a London firm
in cheap form. It is sure to be worth reading.
There is a barn-like Methodist Church in
a Melbourne suburb which has an average congre-
gation of fifty—morning and evening services in-
cluded. The adherents are not millionaires—far
from it, but there is one loyal and liberal member
(who owns a timber-yard, and any number of
houses) who is the mainstay of the Bethel, and
whose devout “Amins,” accompanied by a
peculiar snort, are well known. In fact, he is a
downright generous supporter. It is alleged that
he donates 12s. to the collection every Sunday,
not in coin but I.O.XJ.’s. It must be borne in
mind that the long-suffering pastor of the barn-
like church lives in a house in an evil-smelling side
street, said house belonging to the rich member.
At the end of the quarter, matters are squared up,
1.0.U.’s taken into account, and the balance (not
much) after the rent is docked, handed over tithe
church-treasurer. “The Lord loveth a cheerful
giver.” There was a public meeting in the church
one evening, and the liberal member addressed a
few words to the assembled multitude. In
the course of his rambling utterances, he said that
he had had a “ vision,” in which he handed over
something like £3OO, that being about half the
amount due on the church and parsonage, pro-
viding the congregation “visioned” the other
half. There was not the slightest prospect of the
latter being accomplished, and the chairman of
the meeting suggested that the liberal member
should go home and have another “vision,” so as
to make up the whole amount. However, up to
the present, The Bulletin learns that no further
“ visions ” have been indulged in, and the dimly-
lighted sacred edifice, with its sad and forlorn-
looking congregations, pursues the same old, even
tenor of its way, and the liberal member (who, by
the way, owns a couple of pubs.) still drops Ids
1.0.U.’s into the plate, and still deducts the rent
of the house in the eUI-smelling street. Will it
be written of him that he hath “done all the good
that he could,” or “ he might have done more if
lie would?”
Round the Shops and Shows,
Without a doubt, the best ladies’ dining-
room in Sydney is the City Buffet, corner of
Margaret and George streets. This handsome
room opens off the main street, and has the
advantage of being on a level with the street —a
convenience which is much appreciated by the
ladies. A splendid dinner is served at a cost of
Is. upwards, and, as the table appointments and
attendance are also excellent, the popularity of
the room is only what can be expected.
# * *
Since Princess Beatrice included the art
of photography amongst her pursuits, it has been
one of the most fashionable pastimes adopted by
young ladies and gentlemen in both England and
other parts of the world. The youth of Sydney
have not been slow in having themselves initiated
in the mysteries of this interesting art, for our
young amateur photographers number “quite a
crowd.” Time was, however, when in Sydney
the necessary paraphernalia meant so large a sum
of money, that it debarred scores from indulging
in this useful and fascinating accomplishment, but
since Messrs. Baker and Rouse have come amongst
us, they offer a photographer’s outfit at so trifling
an outlay that it is within the reach of all. This
firm (successors to Messrs. Lichtner and Co.) have
cameras in all sizes, from the tiny “Detective”
upwards, and are to be found at 375 Geprge-street.
next A.J.S. Bank.

Pending the erection of their new premises
in Pitt-street, Messrs. E. Way and Company have
removed to 448 George-street, late Messrs. Pain
and Webb and Co., and directly opposite Messrs.
Lassetter’s, where they have a magnificent display
of spring goods. These temporary premises have
been fitted up on a most liberal scale, the show-
rooms being the most spacious and best-lighted
in Sydney. On the same floor as the show-rooms,
cosy apartments have been reserved for ladies’
“ fitting-on rooms and on the next floor are the
numerous workrooms, always filled with busy
workers. As Messrs. E. Way and Company made
a clearance of their old stock before entering their
George-street premises, it follows that all the
goods they are now showing are the latest im-
portations. They have also opened a branch of
their well-known mourning and underclothing
departments at 430 George-street, next to the
Book Arcade. This Firm has been too long and
favourably known to the Sydney public to necessi-
tate a detailed notice of their many excellent
qualities. Suffice it to say—and this speaks
volumes—they do the biggest drapery business in
Sydney. # # #
Amongst the attractions in the new Arcade
is the “ Imperial Refreshment Room,” conducted
by Mrs. Ridout. Here one can have an excellent
cup of tea with bread and butter and pastry.
Fruit and cream, and the best of sweets are other
specialities at this establishment —in fact, every-
thing in the way of light refreshment is here
offere d. Good attendance is also guaranteed.
# * *
The Ladies’ Self Help Institute has been
removed from 18 Beale’s Chambers to No. 13
Imperial Arcade. The ladies of Sydney cannot
be too strongly urged to give orders for work of all
descriptions at this worthy institution. They will
not be out of pocket by so doing, and will greatly
benefit their own sex.
» # *
All honour to the man who invents a
labour-saver for women. “McFie’s cold-water
soap ” economises both time, fire, and labour and
turns the ever-dreaded washing-day into a day of
pleasure. Its advantages over other soaps are
numerous. In using it for washing clothes no fire
is necessary, no rubbing is required, and the
hands, instead of becoming hardened, become soft
and white. The price is just a fraction over that
of common soap, and it can be bought from any
grocer.
* * *
Mr. Leon Harris, who is one of the prin-
cipal gem-cutters in Sydney, notifies that he offers
the largest price for onals and pearls in the rough.
Mr. Harris also transforms old-fashioned jewellery
into any desired modern style most skilfully.
Artistic diamond-setting is here another speciality.
The address is Market street, between Hunter’s
Boot Palace and Evening News office.

Dr. P. Stanich has removed his consulting-
rooms to 20 Wynyard-square, where he can be
consulted daily. "Dr. Stanich will devote his
attention to the deserving poor for ear, throat and
nose diseases every Tuesday.

Brizard’s rum, and immediately everything will
become tinged with a roseate glow, not to be des-
cribed by words. Try it.

Sydney Echo prints “Facts Focussed.”
Probably a misprint for “ Hocussed.”
An alleged humorous paper, published in
Melbourne, has lately got rid of its editor, who
was the only humorous thing about it, even in its
best days. His proceedings scared the proprietors
Into fits, and the paper is now run by a species of
retired undertaker upon orthodox lines. The old
editor had a turn for poetry, and once a week he
used to blast an appropriate couplet out of him-
self, and issue it along with the weekly cartoon,
and gently label it Byron, or Shakespeare, or even
Homer. He generally did these brief poems in a
tramcar, or he would think them out while running
for a train. Sometimes, by way of variety, he
would issue a mass of hieroglyphics that looked
like a centipede drinking on a barbed-wire fence,
and put underneath the words : “ Obscure Persian
Legend.” But one day, towards the end of his
career, he published a frenzied blast of metre in
two lines, and described it as by “ Sappho,
ancient Greek poetess,” and then a deeply-out-
raged professor came down to the office and
remonstrated. “ You must be aware, Mr. ,”
lie said, sternly, “that the works of Sappho are
entirely lost, and this is mere disreputable fooling
with the great names of antiquity.” The editor
never turned a hair. He only smiled a heavenly
smile and explained. “I know,” he said, “that
the writings of Sappho are supposed to have been
lost, but this single verse was given by her to an
ancestor of mine, and has been handed down in
the family ever since. It is now revealed to the
world for the first time.” Then there was a long
pause, in fact, the pause has been going on ever
since, and is still in good working order.
A final consignment of blackleg shearers
was started along from Melbourne the other day
to be transhipped to the wild and woolly west.
There were about 50 of them, almost all dressed
in the cheap and slimy black togs and the
squashed and mangled hat to which the city-
rowdy is addicted. Six dwarfs, all very drunk —
one intoxicated hunchback, and a knock-kneed
giant whose limbs spread out at an obtuse angle on
each side of him—were the conspicuous features of
the show. He was a reposeful sort of giant, with
a big, dreary countenance, and every time the
caravan stopped in its progress along the plat-
form he would flop down on anything that was
handy. Once he sat on a truckful of baggage, and
a porter started to wheel him away, but some one
ran after him and reclaimed him. It was raining
furiously, and the little man who was in charge of
the consignment rushed about in a sodden state,
and made frantic efforts to verify them by his list
as they came through a wicket. “Here, you
don’t belong to this lot,” he said, as a nigger
shoved past, bent on a free ride, but the mob
behind rushed the nigger through with a yell and
a curse, and swept him into the carriage.
Then the man in charge looked through the
window at the damp, blasphemous crowd, and
tried to count them, but failed. A powerful
smell of muddy swags and foul boots per-
vaded the premises, and a stream of cheerful pro-
fanity poured out over the station. Finally, they
got under weigh, and expectorated out of the
windows all the way to Seymour. At that place
the joyous “ free labourer ” swamped the refresh-
ment-room, and filled the atmosphere with cries
for coffee and pie—“pie, blast you, pass along
some crimson pie; where’s the gory vittles?” The
barmaid fled in confusion, and the mob howled
and seethed and tramped around with elephantine
feet, and reached for the food over other people’s
shoulders, and raised Cain generally. The small
man hung around the outskirts of the tumult, and
counted his flock over again, and found that he
was supplying pie and coffee to about eight more
than the right number. Then he “shoo’d’’them
back into the train and counted them again—
result, six men short. Consequence, fourteen pies
unaccounted for. He found the knock-kneed man
and one intoxicated dwarf at the back of the
station, and fished out the lost hunchback from
behind a truck ; but the nigger, who had howled
the loudest of all in the refreshment-room,
was gone, and so were two more. From
.Seymour to Benalla the crowd still expec-
torated out of the carriage window and
blasphemed, and threw out empty bottles as
before. They were counted again at Benalla—
two more gone—making a total shortage of five.
Presumably they had either evaporated, or escaped
through the window. The others had their muddy
boots on the seat and -were still objurgating
violently, and a good deal of varnish had been
damaged. At Wodonga the shortage had got up
to seven, and getting away from this place one
man put his head out of the window, and cast reflec-
tions on the morals of the entire female popula-
tion. At Albury the agent counted them again
in the refreshment-room and found 12 more than
there were at the beginning—there were just 43
visible and reliable men when the train came in,
and here were 62 swallowing the provender, and
clawing madly for the drinkables, and using
choice language all over the place. While he was
thinking over this discrepancy one of them acci-
dentally charged him in the stomach with the full
weight of his swag, and he gave up. When the
train started once more there were just 41. One
of the absentees was visible on the platform as the
crowd rolled away, but he was in a stooping
posture with his back to the astonished agent, and
his coat-tails over his head. Possibly this atti-
tude was intended to express disrespect. Finally
the whole assortment disembarked at Junee, at
2.30 a.m. It was cold and pitch-dark and raining
in torrents, but by the light of the porter’s lantern
the consignment was once more verified among the
slush; and when the train plunged off on its
weary way into a darkness that was like 10,000
black cats, the tally had got down to 37, and stuck
there.
