SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ Well, you see,” said Larry Foley, “it’s only
to be expected that a man that isn’t doing much would
take on flesh pretty thick—you know. But I was in real
.food fettle that time Simonetti did my bust, you know.”
“Your bust, Larry? Why, I never heard about it.”
“Oh yes, but it wasn't for me, it was for the bishop—you
know, they'd got his head all right, but they wanted to
fix it up so's it ’ud fit well on to a good bust—you know,
so they come to me and, of course, it was a fair deal—you
know, and it come out all right, because everybody said
it was the deadjspit of the bishop—you understand.”
And then an irreverent joker remarked that things had
got mixed up too much and shifted end for end as it
were, and that Larry’s head on the shoulders of a bishop
would do the world a lot more good than the other way
about-you know —and five drinks concluded the business
of the sitting.
# # #
In Timbs’ “ Century of Anecdote ” it is recorded
that the Duke of Wellington, when assailed by the crowd on
pig return from the Tower during the Reform Bill excite-
ment, had a very narrow escape. A young man in a gig
kept close to the Duke’s horse the whole way through the
city in such a manner as to completely guard one side.
He never once looked up, nor had the air or manner of
one who was doing anything out of the way, and we
believe he remains to this day almost unknown, though the
greatest disgrace that could have fallen on the nation was
in all human probability averted by him. It leaked out
some years before his death that this young man was none
other than the somewhat eccentric landlord of the
John Bull tavern, Little Collins-street, Melb., the late
William Barton. The “Kitchen Doctor,” as he was
called, had a varied and somewhat stormy experi-
ence. By birth and' apprenticeship he was a freeman of
the city of London, belonging to the Cordwainers Guild
—and at the time of the excitement over the Reform Bill
was in business as a leather-cutter in Red Cross-street,
Cripplegate Within. This business he disposed of to
enter upon the management of the Rosemary Branch
Garden at Islington. At these gardens he had under
engagement to him Pablo Panque and Tom Barry, the
famous clown, who afterwards visited Australia. At the
Gardens, after four or five years’ occupancy, Barton
was unfortunate, the pavilion, capable of holding
5000 people, circus stables, kennels, horses, dogs and
other exhibits being destroyed by fire. Not having a
penny of insurance on the concern, and all his worldly
wealth being in the venture, Barton, almost penniless,
but full of pluck, emigrated to Victoria, where lie arrived
in the early fifties, engaging in mining at Forest Creek
(now Castlemaine), and various other occupations until
he finally “camped” in Little Collins-street. Barton was
intimately acquainted with the old school of boxers and
theatricals. The snug parlour of his hostelry was always
sought after by old Londoners and others who delighted
in hearing repeated the doings of the “fancy.” Barton
had a wonderful memory and a fund of anecdote not
possessed by many other men of his day in Melbourne.
He bad seen all the great actors from Kemble to Barry
Sullivan, and gave it as his opinion that “Little Edmund
Kean” outstripped them all. In comedy he believed in
Robson, in singing, Braham, and in boxing, Jem Ward,
Young Dutch Sara and Dick Curtis.

The social meeting which took place in America
between Sullivan and Slavin, reminds us of a little narra-
tive by the Tipton Slasher, page 1001 of “ Pugilistica-” :
“ Yer see, the honly meetin’ that ever took place ’atween
Plummy Joe and Balloon was of a most rummy character.
They sot down together in the bar parler, all by their-
selves, and wuz most cheerful. All of a suddent, and
neither of ’em never knowed ’ow it occurred, there was
sich a smashin’ and a crashin as never you heerd in yer
born days. Heverythink in the plaice was smashed. It
all happened in the twinklin’ of a heye. When friends
rushed in, there wuz the two settin’ as companionable as
hever.”

Spagnoletti: “ I saw old Billy Lang to-day,
driving a load of potatoes, from his Ascot Vale Farm,
through Melbourne. Who’s he ? Billy was the crack
jock, of 30 years ago, and more, owner of Flying Jib and
that sort, rival of Sam. Waldock, who had the finest
hands, but Lang had the best cling—to existence, any-
how. He has wrapped his legs, like those of a Fred.
Archer, round Old Father Time.”

* # #
Jere Dunn, the grey-headed, white-whiskered
man who refereed the Slavin-Kilrain bash, is the man who
stalked Elliott, the short-sighted pug. and sworn-off bank
burglar, and to finish a feud shot him “sitting” in
Chicago. Dunn took steady aim at Elliott at five yards’
range from shelter of a screen while his victim was feed-
ing in a Chicago restaurant. Elliott, who had no warning
from his assassin, nearly settled Dunn after being
wounded, and got his gun from him, but was stretched
out with a second revolver, which the other well-knowm
sport had laid on. The facts were abundantly proved in
court, but the defence had more influence and money to
spend than the prosecution, and Dunn got through.

, Melbourne men met with little success at
the Sydney Harriers sports of last Saturday on the Asso-
ciation Ground, only running into the barren honour of
places. The racing provided a number of close finishes
and several dead heats, and in the three-mile race
: ’airytuple put up the Australian record, 16min. ssec.,
though had Corner made the pace stronger during the
last three laps he would probably have wiped out the
eight yards that separated them at the finish. The day
was fine, and all the youth and beauty of Sydpey pimpled
me lawn. Gov. Jersey, placid and devoid of “side,”
wandered round in an “ Andv Kelly ’ and simply
shocking tan shoes. .

# * *
Now that the English cricketers are coming out
once more to this beknighted land, we have little doubt
that Australian democracy will be annoyed and insulted
to see some paid players, Grace to wit, labelled “Mister,”
while others paid less largely are simply styled Tom,
Dick and Harry. In the cricket-field at least surely all
men are equal. The greatest satire on the “gentleman”
qualifications was supplied by the brothers Daft ; one
played as a gentleman, the other as a professional. Both
had been brought up in precisely the same manner.

Mark Toy's scheme (backed up by a donation of
£300) of holding sailing-races over a triangular course in
Sydney harbour (all competing boats’ sails to bear dis-
tinctive colours, and thus to be distinguishable by the
general public), promises to inaugurate a sailing mania.
By a late Russian edict all children, except
the issue of adultery, are legitimised on the after-
marriage of their parents.
Judge Docker has afforded N.S.W. poli-
ticians another chance of reviewing his judicial
character in Parliament, and it is not at all
unlikely, unless he mends his ways, that his eccen-
tricities on the bench may form the subject of a
substantive motion in both Houses.

for musical instruments. The trade, as well as the
public, will do well to roll up to 45 York-street on
the Ist Sept.

THE STRONGEST ARGUMENTS
A recent issue of the Wolverhampton
Chronicle contains the following: “We have
for a long time heard various reports of the
wonderful effects produced by the use of St.
Jacobs oil in chronic cases which had been given
up as incurable, but had always supposed these
were enlarged upon for advertising purposes, like
all other patent or proprietary medicines, but the
reports from all our leading chemists must be
considered one of the strongest arguments which
can be advanced respecting the value of any lead-
ing article of trade in their line. The uniformity
of these reports will be noted at once. All speak
in the highest terms of the value of St. Jacobs oil
as an outward application for pain. The oil is
only advertised to cure rheumatism, neuralgia,
gout, sciatica, and those bodily pains where an
outward application is used. The proprietors of
this popular remedy never advertise the oil to
cure those diseases which it will not actually cure.
By so doing they have gained the confidence of
the people. We see by the Liverpool papers that
St, Jacobs oil is used largely on board of nearly
every steamship that sails from that port. Many
companies, including the Cunard, White Star,
and some of the South American lines, do not
even consider their ships ready for sea unless a
sufficient supply of this popular remedy is on
board to last the voyage. Latterly we are in-
formed that all H.M. troopships leaving Ports-
mouth take a large supply of the oil on board.
The oil in yellow wrappers for veterinary purposes
is being used with equally good results by the
leading omnibus and cab companies of London
a nd provincial cities for use on horses for sprains,
sore shoulders, and general ailments where an
embrocation is required.
“A leading article in a recent issue of the
Chemist and Druggist stated that St. Jacobs
Oil had the largest sale of any medicine in the
world; and when an article possesses the actual
merit which St. Jacobs Oil undoubtedly does
possess, the proprietors are entitled to receive the
benefit of the enormous sale which this popular
remedy has.”
DRAWBACKS.
The electric light is a glorious institution, so is
a donkey race, or a mince pie, an aperient pill,
or a pledge of affection ; but they all have their
little drawbacks, as the proprietors of each or any
of them have but too often realised. They are
all convenient at times, and occasionally comfort-
ing, but the electric light, in addition to being
jerky and unreliable, is unfair to women. The
loveliest of her sex doesn’t show to advantage in its
glare; but soon gets accustomed to it, and learns the
precise shades of color, both for complexion and
costume, that will blend into a soulful harmony be-
neath it, and defy spiteful comment. Masculine
beauty prefers it to daylight, for, though search-
ing it is likewise secretive, by reason of its
attendant shadows, which will help a convicted
thief or a bigamist to pass for an apostle, and
substitute an expression of pensive thought for the
drivelling imbecility produced by colonial whisky
at Is. 6d. a bottle. It is better than candles in
many respects, and inferior to limelight in others,
but it will probably never get as firm a hold on the
affections of the people as its humble congener, the
fat lamp, than which, however, it is less aggressive
in perfume, though far more costly and difficult
of attainment. The fat lamp is man’s trustworthy
and permanent friend wherever there is a pint pot,
a piece of rag, and a pound of loin chops; the
electric light is at the disposal of an engineer who
may be the object of a domestic tyranny which
unmans him during the hours of darkness, or the
subject of an influenza that nothing but onions
boiled in beer will alleviate. Luxene, the new
illuminant, has none of these disadvantages and
no others. It is simply perfect from all points of
study, and —better than all else—it is absolutely
safe to use. The most timid mother can rely on
finding her children in bed when she returns from
a short visit to a neighbor, and the lamp (turned
low) will illuminate the peaceful scene she left an
hour before and there will be no fire and wreckage
to illustrate the story of her absence. Take
note of this and ask for confirmation from
Messrs. J. Barlow and Co., T. W. Eady and
Sop, Mason Bros., Ltd., Tillock and Co., D.
Mitchell and Co., J. Keep and Sons, Holds worth,
M‘Pherson and Co., J. Gr. Hanks & Co., M'Lean
Bros, and Bigs’, Ltd., Fred. Butcher, and Robert
Miller, Sydney; J. Ireland, R. Hall and Sons,
and David Miller, Newcastle.

An ugly Cooktown gin was the other day
offered haif-a-crown if she would kiss a certain
magistrate. She gallantly strove to earn the
money, but the magistrate sent her sprawling.
The London & Sydney Tea Go. supply squatters
and farmers at wholesale prices.
By way of reducing the deficiency a little?
the Victorian authorities propose, in future, to
make every lighthouse-keeper pay rent for his
lighthouse, on the ground that he is supplied with
“ quarters,” and thereby gets an advantage over
other public servants who have to find their own
quarters. It’s a wonder they don’t also charge
board to people in gaol. The rate proposed is
about 10s. a week, which seems pretty steep for
the privilege of living in a stone funnel in a
wilderness of sea and cold grey stones, with
neither a newspaper nor a drink anywhere within
twenty miles or more. It is a melancholy job at
the best to run a lighthouse. Life there consists
chiefly of polishing up the old reflector by day and
keeping an eye on the lantern glare at night, and
in his leisure time the keeper thinks about the
lizards and the damp, or walks around on the
shore jumping on crabs, or else sits and looks out
of his weary eye, amid his uncut hair, at the same
old, green sea that is always there. When his
hair is cut at all he has to do it himself, and he
looks after his own clean shirt, and repairs his
own leg when it is broken, and darns his own
socks, and pulls out his teeth when they ache.
Ten shillings a week seems pretty stiff for a
domicile under these circumstances, with no bath-
room, and no water laid on, and no gas-fittings,
cornices, front garden, or trams passing the door
—with nothing at all, in fact, except a round stone
hole and a hunk of the illimitable ocean.
The legion cures performed by the patented
treatment of the Bethel Electric Medical Baths
Co., Lonsdale-street, Melbourne, increase with the
advent of every new disease treated thereat. A
Sandhurst man, who had been totally blind
through optical paralysis, had his vision restored
to him after six baths, and a lady—a martyr to
rheumatism for six years—has been enabled to
walk after being under treatment for about three
weeks. There is not the slightest doubt that a
prssage'of electricity through diseased tissues
arrests decay and re-tones the whole system, as
dozens of cases have authoritatively evidenced.
And when it is remembered that the process is
more pleasurable than otherwise—that “ the
medicine, in fact, is a sweetmeat”—the tre-
mendous spread of the Company’s business is not
at all to be wondered at. Dr. H. B. Wilson, the
medical superintendent, personally supervises the
force of the currents in the dynamo-room, and
attendants of both sexes are to hand in the various
bathrooms. Altogether, the Bethel Electric
Baths must be considered the wonder, and at the
same time the saviour of the health, of this age.

The Victorian Government spends rather
over £lOO,OOO per annum on printing solemn
literature, departmental reports, and pompous
statistics of all sorts. One of their joyous publxa-
tions is the report of the Reformatory Schools
and Neglected Children’s department—a big
annual volume in which the State reproduces the
letters of little Tommy, the typical infant who is
boarded out by the authorities, to his drunken
mother; and similar information. Here is a
sample :
Dear Mother,—l write these few lines hoping to find
you well, and not as you were before. I hope that God
will protect you till I return again. Mind and keep out
of danger, and I will never forget you as long as I live.
Mind 3'our religion, and don’t drink beer. lam going to
school every day. lam third in the class at M . I
have a comfortable house, and plenty of food. Trust in
God, and wait till I return. Your Lovixa Sox.
There are several dozens of these in each issue of
this famous blue-book, and little Tommy snuffles
in every one of them. Why the State pays for
publishing the correspondence of the oily moral
infant who is boarded out, goodness only knows.
But the letters are all so much alike that we are
half inclined to suspect that the department is its
own infant, or rather that there is no infant at all,
and the officials get up the correspondence them-
selves to show the good that is being done under
their supervision, and pass it off as being written
by a reformed and beautiful child with a neglected
nose, who is sorrowing for his ma.

HEAD OFFICE: CREEK ST., BRISBANE.
pROSPECTUb.
OF THE
Melbourne Hotel Company,
STRAHAN, TASMANIA.
(To he Registered No-Liability.)
CAPITAL :
£12,01K), in 12,001) Shares of £1 Each.
Payable as follows : —Five Shillings per Share ou
Application; Five Shillings per Share on
Allotment; and the Balance by Calls, the
First Call not to be made earlier than Six
Months after Allotment of Shares.
This company is being formed for the purpose
of buying from William Dixon, Esq., a portion
of land situate at Strahan, West Coast of Tas-
mania, containing 1 rood 31 perches, and erecting
a most substantial Hotel and Store thereon. Title
perfect.
The object of the company in buying this
valuable land and building a Hotel on the same,
is, that a Hotel of this class in this particular
district is indispensable, and that people of all
classes going to and from those wonderful mines
in Tasmania—Zeehan, Dundas, and Mount Lyell,
&c.— must necessarily pass the above-mentioned
Hotel.
The proposed building is to consist of Forty-
five Bedrooms, all well-ventilated, Sitting-rooms,
Drawing-rooms, Billiard-room, Bar 18 x 24, and
to possess all conveniences connected with a
Modern Hotel. The Kitchen will be on the
ground floor, connecting with the Dining-room, so
that no inconvenience will be experienced in the
delivery of ineals. A verandah Bft. wide will run
almost around the entire building.
The structure, when completed, will command a
prominent position in Strahan, also a most exten-
sive view of Macquarie Harbour, the scenery of
which is unsurpassed; being also in the very
midst of every conceivable sport a tourist could
wish for—Shooting, Fishing, Yachting, Boating,
&c.
The proposed structure will be about six chains
from the sea, where a bathing place, from one
inch to fifty feet, can be acquired.
It is proposed that W. Dixon, Esq., owner of
the within-mentioned land, shall receive £5OO
cash, and 1000 shares paid up to 10s. for the said
land, and that the building shall be of brick, and
shall not cost more than £OOOO.
The expenses of flotation will be borne by the
company, and the company will be declared
formed when application for 6000 shares has been
made.
The special attention of investors is directed to
the fact that the district in which the within-
mentioned Hotel is to be built is unsurpassed,
being a most salubrious climate, and admirably
suited for invalids ; also, the tourist can have
every enjoyment conceivable. The population of
Strahan and surrounding district is about 8000.
In conclusion, we would again draw special atten-
tion to the fact that there is no other outlet pos-
sible from Zeehan, Dundas, Mount Lyell, &c.,
except through the seaport of Strahan, and past
the proposed Hotel, which will be in the closest
possible proximity to the Zeehan-Dundas Railway
Station and Terminus,
All money received for application of shares
will be placed to the credit of the company in the
Commercial Bank, Melbourne; and, if no allot-
ment is made, the deposit money will be returned
without any deduction whatever.
Temporary Office : 330 Collins Street.
J. T. RAWLTNSON,
Secretary pro teiu.
Brokers ; FORD & WALSH,
Mercantile Chambers, Collins Street.
Share List closes 14 days after expiration of the
above advertisement.
258 Drummond-st., Carlton, May 13, 1891.
Mr< Wm. Dixon, Osborne House,
N icholson-street.
Dear Sir, —At your request, on my return from
reporting on various properties on the West Coast
of Tasmania, I made another special inspection of
your corner allotment No. 1, situate at the town or
seaport of Strahan, Macquarie Harbour, and in
close proximity to the terminus of Zeehan, Dundas
and Strahan line of railway. As I have before
informed you, it is without doubt the best situa-
tion in the locality to build a Hotel and Store
that can be desired. There being now no doubt
as to the stability and every day increasing
returns of the Zeehan and Dundas silver fields,
or the “Tasmanian Broken Hill;” and, speak-
ing without prejudice, and as a frequent visitor
since 1886, backed by every other expert and
the present and ex-Government geologists and
mining inspectors, etc., I confidently recom-
mend this property for investment, and should
you determine to make a company, I am preparer}
to take my share. The salubrity of this climate is
at all times unsurpassed. Protected by Native
from every wind (winter or summer), and as a
summer resort for invalids or the tourist, it cannot
be denied no place in the Australian colonies can
surpass it. Its pure atmosphere and splendid
natural drainage makes it a safe anchorage from
the ailments subject to the natural body. The
tourist can satisfy his every fancy. The scenery
of Macquarie Harbour is unsurpassed—in point
of fact an inland sea, beaming in beauty ; its
islands, its vast mountain rivers, and silver
streams, studded with foliage of every description.
The Gordon River, to wit, upon its charming
banks; the towering forests and marble cliffs
150fc., and the inland side behold the trickling
stream and gurgling torrent descending into
many a deep ravine and abyss below ; then,
onward still, and the noble Gordon takes you up to
the settlement of 100 years ago, &c. I could
expatiate a week oh the marvels of Creation
visible there. The visitor can have every enjoy-
ment-fishing (supply wonderful of every kind),
shooting (wild fowl), &c., &0., and a natural
bathing-place—about six chains from allotment—
from lin. to 50ft. I strongly recommend you to
build a hotel costing from £3OOO to £5000.* Just
consider—material on ground, an ever-increasing
population, close to railway, close to steamer, no
position like it, and not half accommodation for
travelling public. The population is now about
8000, including surrounding district, and no other-
outlet. It is a fortune to build a suitable place.
In conclusion, there is no outlet for Mount Lyell,
Mounts Zeehan, Dundas, &c., &c., except through
Strahan,
I am, yours truly,
GERALD FITZGERALD,

NOBODY WANTS THAT GOLD RING.
For nearly 100 years a certain family of vvorking
people living in Pans have ended their lives by
suicide. From father to son, from mother to
daughter, has descended a plain gold ring, and on
the linger of every one of these suicides, as they
lay in death, this ring has been found. Only last
year the body of a young man who had killed
himself was brought to the Morgue, and on his
linger was the fatal golden circlet, he was the
last of his race. The ring was buried with the
corpse, from which no one acquainted with its
history will have the courage to remove it.
The mental taint in this family came from some
remote ancestor, and was intensified by their
recognition of it until it became a controlling
force : and the ring was accepted as imposing upon
its possessor the obligation to commit suicide,
after the example of the nersqn who last wore it.
This form of mania usually originates in a disorder
of the nervous system, which'in its turn arises
from anaemia, or poverty of the blood, one of the
results of imperfect nutrition.
A recent letter from a gentleman living in Nor-
folk contains the following assertion : “ J longed
for death; I was afraid of the night; I was afraid
to he alone, yet I hated society. I was afraid that
in some one of these hours oj deep gloom and depres-
sion I should lift my hand against my own life, for
I knew that many had done so from the same cause.”
The dark hours became a time of terror to him, so
he says. He tossed and tumbled on his bed,
wondering if morning would ever dawn again. In
this case it was not an accusing conscience, as he
had committed no offence ; the cause was purely
a physical one —yet, all too common in England—
indigestion and dyspepsia, with the long chain of
consequences dragging after it, nervous collapse
among them. .
He relates that his skin and eyes had been more
\>r less discoloured for years, often of a ghastly
j a d repulsive yellow. This was due to the presence
of bile in the blood and tissues, where it had no
business to be. But as the weak and torpid liver
could not remove it, no other result was possible
than the one our friend experienced. His head
frequently ached as though fiends had turned it
into a workshop, and pains chased one another
through his body as though he had at least half
the maladies catalogued in the popular books of
disease.
Yet one thing, and one only, was responsible
for all the mischief, namely, the poison introduced
into the blood from the decaying food in the
stomach and intestines. Ihe cold feet, the loss
of appetite and ambition, the mental despondency,
the sense of weariness and fatigue, the bad taste
iu the mouth, dry cough, giddiness, palpitation,
chills, weakness, &c., are a brood of foul birds
hatched in one nest, and the mother is always
indigestion and dyspepsia.
Time passed somehow, as it always does,
whether we laugh or cry, and this man grew
heartily tired of a life thus burdened and spoiled.
He longed to see the end of it, and no wonder.
But the last page of his letter is pitched in a
higher key. He says, “ When I think of what
I was, and what I. am now, I can hardly realise
the change. For the past six months I have been
using a preparation known as Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup, and it has actually revolutionised
my whole system. One of my tenants recom-
mended it to me, and I tried it just to please him.
Now I praise it for myself, and thank the men
who made and advertise it. My troubles are over,
and I feel (at 57) as light, elastic, and gay as a
boy on his summer vacation. I tell my doctors
they are beaten at their own trade by an old
German nurse, and so far as I am concerned they
can’t deny it. I have no more horrible thoughts
of self-destruction, for I find too much enjoyment
in living. My thanks are too deep for words.”
The author of this letter consents to the pub-
lication of so much of it as is here printed, but
declines to allow the use of his name, at least for
the present, for reasons we are bound to respect.
But the evident sincerity of his story will carry
conviction to every candid mind.

The Fourness Lamp, unlike most of the
so-called regenerative lamps, is, from first to last,
the actual manufacture of the company. The
undoubted advantage of this is that every part is
subjected to rigid scrutiny, and finished in the
best possible manner. The Fourness Lamp
claims to be absolutely perfect in its brilliant
purity and steady intensity of light. There is_no
downward shadow or reflection. It is simplicity
itself to light and clean, is readily fixed to exist-
ing supply pipes, and yields more than treble the
results obtained by ordinary methods of lighting,
with less gas consumption. It is smokeless, and
only gives out a heat sufficient to insure thorough
ventilation and absolute purity of atmosphere.
The security it affords from danger of fire, has
made it a general favourite with the Insurance
Companies. The Company is actively engaged in
producing the new Shadowless Bracket or Burner,
a light specially adapted for shops, offices, and
private houses complete in itself, and can be easily
secured to any existing brackets, pendants or
chandeliers by merely removing the burner, in
place of which it may be fixed in a moment with-
out even the aid of a gasfitter. It is little inferior
to the large Pendant Lamp and elegant in appear-
ance, consumes but little gas in comparison with
the immense volume of light given and is smoke-
less, cool, and simple in construction.
Nat Levi, the Melbourne advertising
agent, has recklessly offered himself to hi**
adopted country (from the hustings) on severa 1
occasions, but the goal of his ambition seems to get
further away each time. The large majority of
the constituents display a fatuous desire to hurl
their votes and interest at the other fellows, and
when the returning-officer comes forth with a
sheet of paper in his nervous grasp, the announce-
ment of Nat’s discomfiture is accepted with patient
resignation and an ironical guffaw. The other
week, when Mr. Pitt was returned for Beaney’s
old seat in the Council, poor Nathaniel was so
hopelessly beaten off that his £40 deposit reverted
to the revenue, which is just now glad to grab at
all it can get. Yet Nat, also his comrade in mis-
fortune, Derbin Willder, can be warranted to
return at the next opportunity, with the same old
lot of broad, liberal views. Willder seems to have
a considerable pull over the other splendid failure,
inasmuch as he always comes up smiling, where-
as the smile of Nathaniel is such a gruesome affair
that he is carefully advised to keep it in the back-
ground as much as possible. Fate has denied him
the chance of ever passing into the High Court of
Parliament, on his face.

The average life of the “ fallen women
of London, dating from the day when they join
the “ unfortunate ” class to the day of their death,
is just 3| years. What they die of isn’t very well
defined, but hunger and bad liquor are responsible
for a lot of the mortality—mostly hunger. The
business doesn’t pay. Most of the subjects come
to grief morally before they are twenty, and die
of rouge and wet feet and old age and general
break-up of the system before they are 24. London
alone uses up a good many thousands of them
every year, but the supply never runs short all
the same, the trade is ruined by over-competition,
or Freetrade, or freedom of contract, or some-
thing—anyhow there is no money in it, and no
prospects, except prospects of fog and gin. A
large proportion of the unfortunates drop into the
Thames, but they are often fished out by interfer-
ing persons with hooks, and run in, and talked to
death by big, hide-bound females with bags and
tracts. A collection will now be taken up to
promote the spread of Christianity among Christ-
ians, and to convert bishops and preachers.

His temper changed once more. “Why do you
ask ? Do you want to give me up like that damneTd
jade did, and get the reward !” And then the
insane suspiciousness fled again before his need for
human sympathy, and he held out the hand which
clasped the book to the boy.
“ Here —take it ! I’ll trust you. Open and read.
Strike a light first!
The lad, unwillingly enough, obeyed. He had
never been with the old man after nightfall, and
this evening there was an unusual aspect of wild-
ness and uncanniness about him. But his
curiosity overcame his trepidation, and held him
to the spot. He lit the candle in a wall-sconce,
which was the sole light-giving appliance of the
room, and by its flickering proceeded to read the
contents of the volume.
“ Aloud !” whispered the old man. And aloud
the boy read the first page—an “ absconding
notice,” cut, as an appended note in manuscript
testified, from a Sydney Gazette. The notice
was as follows :—
Principal Superintendent of Convicts’ Office,
Sydney, May 17, ,182-.
rpiIE undermentioned Prisoners, having absented them-
selves from their respective Employments, and some
of them at large with false Certificates, all Constables and
others are hereby required to use their utmost exertions
in lodging them in safe custody.
Any person harbouring, concealing, or maintaining any
of the said absentees will be proseeuted for the oflence.
(Signed) F. A. Hely, J.P.,
Principal Superintendent.
A list of the absconders followed, some 30 or 40,
and the boy was reading it down. “ Go down to
the ‘S’s’!” interrupted the old man. And read
what you see. Third of the “S’s” the boy read
out the particulars of William Shovey.
“ Shovey—William, Henry Porcher, labourer,
19, Dublin, 5ft. 5|in., chestnut eyes, brown hair,
brown complexion, from No. 9 Iron Gang.”
' “Who’s William Shovey?” cried the terrible
old man. He beat his hairy breast with savage
irritation. “ Who’s Shovey, William ? Say, you
whelp, say ! Don’t stand there like a fool, but
answer.”
“You are!” the boy replied, startled with
sudden prescience.
“Yes, I am!—I am William Shovey, and I
don’t care who knows it now, for my time is
coming soon ! You know it now, and Johnny
Fawkner did, and you’re the only two in all Vic-
toria who know it. But I don’t care now—I’ll
advertise it in the Age to-morrow, and the Argus
that. I’m William Shovey—ha, ha !”— the misery
of that laughter !—“that would be funny, wouldn’t
it, to advertise oneself as an absconder? But,
you, go on ! See what’s on the next page !”
Then the boy read this : —

On the back of the document—it was pasted on a
guard so that the endorsement could bo read easily
—were Dillon’s particulars : —
Dillon, William, Surry (2), Labourer, 20,

“ And who’s Dillon?” questioned the ex-convict.
“ Who’s Dillon, whelp?”
“ You are, I suppose. But I won’t stop hereto
be called whelp.” The youngster turned to go.
“Don’t go, boy! You’ll be sorry when you
know rny time’s come. I won’t call you whelp
again. But who’s Dillon ? ”
“ If you are not, I don’t know.”
“ But if I’m Dillon, of the Surry Second, how
is it I’m Shovey of the Henry Porcher?”
The boy was beginning to know the System,
and so he fired a shot which was not altogether a
random one.
“ You stole Dillon’s certificate of freedom when
you bolted!”
It was as though the old man had been trying
an experiment, the result of which was destructive
to himself. He flamed into mad violence of speech
and gesture, and denounced the boy.
“ Like all the rest, traitors and spies! You use
the knowledge you gtt from me to convict me.
Damn you ! You piece things together to put
me away ! If you can make me out to be Shovey,
free on a false certificate, others can, the police
can, the judge can !”
But the boy, angered as well as alarmed at the
man’s eccentricity, stopped to hear no more. He
passed out, and never saw Dillon, otherwise
Shovey, again.
But ten days afterwards there came to his
father’s address a manuscript, which, in feeble
scrawl, on blue paper, made the ex-convict’s
peace,

Tales of the Old Regime.
No. XIII.-The "Henry Porcher" Bolter.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Within a stone’s throw of the house, in
Church-street, Richmond, Victoria, wherein Peter
Lalor died, is a cottage which is as ancient a
building as you will find in the suburbs of
young Melbourne. Dilapidated inside, weather-
stained as to exterior, for years it has been
without tenants for longer periods than it has
enjoyed them. It would have had fewer still, had
its landlord’s agent informed intending lessees of
the eerie legend of the place.
For hero it was that one of the “bolters,”
advertised by Mr. F. A. Hely, J.P., Principal
Superintendent of Convicts, in the Sydney
Gazette, in the year 1827, was found (ne morn-
ing, in the Early Seventies, stark-stiff, dead.
* * *
How the writer came to know him need
not here be related. Suffice it that between us
there was an intimacy of a kind. He tolerated
the school-boy ; and the school-boy regarded him
with something of respect and more of fear. For
he was not a loveable character.
Two services did the ex-convict dp to the school-
boy. He introduced him to the System and the
Regime—a service which was of very questionable
value. And he made him free of a marvellous
collection of books. As to which privilege also
the writer has his doubts. If he had not had per-
mission to ramble at will through the Bolter’s
books, he might have developed into a portly and
wealthy banker, instead of a hard-up journalist.
This last remark is not altogether by the way.
Had it not been for the “Bolter ” and his books
probably these “Tales of the Regime” would
never have been written.
# # #
His books were everywhere in the house.
From the hall-entrance to the back-door, from the
basement to the attic ceiling, they were filed in
lobbies and rooms, on stair-cases and in fire-
places. They blocked window-recesses and" ob-
scured sky-and-fan lights. Narrow passages, the
breadth of a man’s frame, wormed through the
heaps of volumes, so that he could make his way
to any particular class of work desired. For he
preserved amidst apparent chaos a system of
arrangement, and though he absolutely dispensed
with the use of shelving, he could, without diffi-
culty, find a book when he wanted it.
Literally surrounded by his books, he lived the
life of a solitary. But one person was admitted to
the house—the aforesaid school-boy. Even the
carrier who brought home the parcels of his pur-
chases from Dwight, the bookseller’s, or Shibb,
the auctioneer’s, was never allowed an inch beyond
the threshold, and, however weighty the package,
or miscellaneous its contents, the old biblomaniac
would drag it in himself. Tradesmen never came
to the house, for he himself bought in the shops
and prepared his food, and the postman, when he
brought a rare letter, knew better than to knock
or ring. He would slip it under the door, and the
old man might or might not pick it up. Which-
ever he did, it was all one in the result, for the
letter was never opened. After his death a letter
from Quaritch, London, dated five years before,
was found, offering him a four-figure sum for a
unique Bible known to be in his possession. Sir
Redmond Barry bought the treasure for a song at
the sale.
His very bed supported books; the table on
which he took his spare meals Was partly covered
with them, and the seat he generally favoured in
preference to the one chair to be discovered on the
premises was a low pile of some folios bound in
the sturdy fashion past generations were wont to
entomb their Dryasdusts. He lived in his books,
and died among them.
His name was, as the boy knew it first, William
Dillon, and he told it to the school-boy in the
rainy duskiness of a December evening. The boy
had stolen in by the back-way to say good-bye
before leaving for up-country to spend his Christ-
mas holidays. And the old man, for the first
time and the last time, welcomed him with soft-
ness of voice. Usually he had but one tone, the
raucous surliness of the hater of the human.
“So you are going before my time comes, lad ?”
“Sir!” for the boy did not understand the
allusion.
“My time is soon now. Johnny Fawkner’s
gone, and I said I would not be long after him !”
“Did you know Mr. Fawkner, sir? I never
heard you speak of him before !”
“Know him? Of course! It was he who
helped me in Hobart Town after my bolt from the
gang. ”
The boy listened. The old man had told a
hundred times of the colonial System in the brave
.days of old, but in all his narratives he had sought
to convey that his knowledge was that only of
the student and the observer. That he had owned
a personal interest in the System flashed into the
boy’s mind.
“Do you not hear, you whelp ! Did you hear
me use the word gang ?”
He was lying on his bed. Between his body and
the wall were books, and books were at his feet.
As he spoke he, vexed, half-rose, and in the petu-
lance of his movement, thrust some of the volumes
to the floor, or rather upon other books.
The boy stooped to pick them up.
“ Stop !” roared the old man, with an accent as
of near thunder. “ Stop ! How dare you touch
it!”
The lad held in his hand a small book, elegantly
bound in calf, with rich toolings and deep-gilt
edges. It was the first he had picked up. Before
he could answer, the old man spoke again.
“ Give it to me, you young rascal! Isthathpw
you treat me? But why should you be different ?
You’re all alike you, men, women and chil-
dren, traitors all, spies all !” He snatched the
book and thrust it between the laps of his open
vest, and cursed the boy again.
The lad grew indignant, though his awe of the
old fellow was such as"to prevent his uttering his
feelings with the characteristic rudeness of the
“ young colonial.” All he cared to say was:
“ I’m no traitor or spy ! I. don’t know what the
book is, and I don’t want to know. I’m off now,
so good night!”
The rumble gave place to a strange pleading.
“ Don’t go, lad, don’t. I was too sharp. And
don't mind me, for my time is very close, very
close !” He paused, and a gasping sigh parted
the gathering darkness, and thrilled the lad fear-
somely.
“ My time's coming close, and there’s no one to
do justice to —to ”
“ To whom ? ” the lad ventured to ask, at last.
“ You should not have left me that night,
my boy. You should have put up with my non-
sense. For I was mad.
“ But, as you went away, don’t come back.
You offended me by going, and I never forgive an
offence.
“But I will tell you now that I am Shovey, and
that I did steal Dillon’s certificate of freedom. 1
tell you so that in 20 years’ time you can tell
others. Not under 20 years —that will see the
last of Dillon. For Dillon is still alive. Every
week a letter comes to me in care of Dwight, from
Gleadow, the solicitor, in Launceston, which tells
me whether he is alive or dead. He was alive last
week.
“All along I have meant to do him justice.
But he has life-sentence after life-sentence, and I
could not do him good. Gleadow has tried for-
me, but as I did not tell Gleadow all the truth, he
could do nothing. All I could do was to pay a
warder at Port Arthur to give him tobacco fre-
quently. I don’t know whether the warder gave
him all I paid for. But I do know that once some
of the stuff was found upon him, and he got 20
lashes.
“Yes, Dillon’s alive, and he will outlive me.
And, if he does, he will have every penny 1
possess. All will be sold, and I will leave such
papers—they are ready now—that surely the Tas-
manian Government will grant him release.
“ He shall have wealth and ease at the last. Mu

•‘lt Was in 1819 that I first knew Dillon. We
Were in the Parramatta Road iron-gang together,
He was a softy then, did his work as well as ho
could to please the overseer, and being afraid of
the rest of us, we put upon him. But he had his
revenge.
“ When he was freed, and the very day he got
his certificate, he came down to the gang; Because
he had pleased, the overseer he let Dillon conle
near us and speak to us. Any other man would
have been arrested. But Dillon spoke to us, and
taunted us, and we could not say a word in reply,
“He held the certificate in his mawly, and he
came near to each one of us and thrust it under
our eyes. ‘There,’ he would say, ‘you ain’t got
that! You’re lags, and I’m a free man.’ And we
durst not say anything, though wo were maddened
at the thought of it.
“ Th- re were nineteen of us all told, and wo
were caged at night in the watch-house-yard,
George-street toll-bar. An open shed it was we
were in, but ’twas better far than Barracks—
there wasn’t a warder or a guard on the inside,
From look-up to rising we were left to ourselves,
And the deviltry that went on then !
“ But that night there was no deviltry ; we
Were all too keen to discuss Dillon and his dirty
trick in coining and insulting us. We were under
Government and in irons, but ’twas no reason
why we should be insulted. We had a little of
men’s feelings left, we said one to another, and we
would not stand it.
“When we got our ration, instead of beginning
the devils’row, as usual, we were quiet—so quiet
that the sentry poked in his head and said he was
sure we were a-brewing mischief-. We told him
we were all dog-tired, that was Why, and he was
satisfied.
We talked and talked what we should do to
Dillon, and at last we decided to steal his paper,
But who should steal it?—that was the question,
“ Someone said we should fight for it, we were
nine couples and a single, and the couples should
fight; each two men against another two, and
the winners should fight one another. At the
end of the first round the bye-man should be
taken into the lot, and someone else should stand
out. So for the third round and till the last.
And the victor in the last ties should fight the
bye-man, and the best man of the two should bo
helped to make a bolt of it, so that he might steal
Dillon’s certificate.
“ It was a good idea, we agreed, and a happy
way of spending Sunday. For the next day was
Sunday, and not being in barracks we were not
mustered for church; We Would be mustered at
morning ration, and that Was all. So We could
haVe the day for sparring, and the winner could
bolt at night.
“We slept quiet that night, and the sentry
told the overseer who mustered us that he was
sure there was devilment afoot, and the overseer
sent down to the Military Barracks for another
sentry. That did not trouble us.
“At nine o’clock we paired off. And at half-
past nine I had put my man down ; he was older
than I, but knew more about sparring, and our
battle was the longest.
“ At ten o’clock we drew fresh lots, and I was
fortunate enough to stand out the third round,
That was one freedom, I am sure, that resting,
The others went at it, and by this time the news
had spread, and numbers of persons, some on
their way to church, stopped to peer through the
half-opened gates some gave the sentries a dump
a-head to come inside and watch us closely.
“ In the fourth round I was drawn, but my
man was soon done up, for he had fought all
through, and then I had to fight the bye-man. It
was awful, that tussle, for he was nearly my own
age and strength. But I knew a trick
which threw him, and when his head fell
on the cobbles, it dazed him, and he
could not come up to time. Then the persons in
the yard made a subscription fot itlfi, ahd gitVe tide
tvV'd dollars and tike dumps.* But the sentries
tdOk half.
“ That night I bolted. I was helped over the
wall at the rear where there were no sentries, by
some of the gang. The others were making the
customary row, quarrelling and fighting among
themselves, so as to deceive the guards.”
* * *
Here Shovey’s manuscript broke off. He
caused it to be left at the boy’s home a day or two
before he died—by whom was not known. He
must have felt that “ his time was coming,” and
penned the narrative perhaps to facilitate the
doing of tardy justice to—his victim,
The boy thought the story was ended, when, oil
showing the papers to his elders, enquiries were
made, and it Was found that Dillon Had died at
Fort Arthur on the eVe of that Christmas which
had been Shovey’s last.
Ytars afterwards, however, he heard from one
who knew.both men, the sequel of the escape.
‘ Shovey did not tell you the whole of the cir-
eumstances preliminary to his bolt. He omitted
one most material particular,
“ That was that he was to return and show the
others of the gang the stolen certificate.”
“ What good could thatdothem?”
“None, But it Would prove to them that
Shovey had carried out the agreement on which
alone they had helped him to escape; for which
they received punishment. They were, every man
Jack of them, flogged for not informing the
guard. Well, Shovey did not return, and as their
notion had been less to free Shovey than to punish
Dillon, they felt betrayed, and vowed the convict
oath to revenge themselves upon him. And,
somehow or other, in that mysterious way in
which intelligence spread in the old days, among
the whole penal population, Shovey learnt of their
resolve.”
“ But how did he steal the certificate ? ”
“There was at that time in Sussex-street,
Sydney, an inn, notorious for being the resort of
absconders. Why the watch left it undisturbed
for so many years would be a puzzle, if it were
not known that they were always bribable. To
this place, Shovey went, and being a likely fellow
—that is, likely to pay if not recaptured—Whs
aided in his design to escape,to Vati Diemert’s
Land. He would have’ gone without attempting
to steal the certificate from Dillon, but the landlord
of the ‘ public ’—influenced by the Freemasonry
of scoundrelism—swore that if he did not commit
the theft, he would not only not assist him to
leave the colony, but would hand him over to the
police.
“ Well, he stole the certificate. A girl was
kept at the public-house to act as a decoy-duck,
and, by her wiles, Dillon was drawn to the place,
Necessarily he had his certificate with him ; and
it was the easiest mode to hocus the man, and rob
him of his ‘papers.’ You see, Dillon was not
likely ; he intended, if he could, to remain ‘square,’
and the publican, who was also a ‘ fence,’ hoped to
make a decent profit from Shovey.
“ Shovey got off with the certificate to Hobart
Town. There’, a few pricks with the needle and a
little gunpowder made him tally with Dillon’s
description. Young Johnny Fawkner, believing
lum to be Dillon helped him to work.
He did well for a year or two. Then, one
d^’r. t -n ere^ n u dup k fche wom . an - . ,
Dillon had been flung out into the street after
the theft, hocussed, drunk; At a Word ftottl the
publican, the Watch obligingly removed him to
the cells, and next day (as a newly-freed prisoner
unworthy of freedom because ho had lost his
papers)—these were but Alexander Berry’s words
—he was awarded 12 months’ gang-work. What
wonder he was desperate? What wonder he now
committed crimes? Before, the 12 months were
expired he was under sentence for life.
“Then, the woman relented, she knew that
others of No. 9 Gang had brooded over Shovey’s
treachery themselves, as they regarded it, and
were eager to avenge it. They thought nothing
of the wrong to Dillon ; what they resented was
the fancied wrong to themselves. The girl,
repentant of her share for Dillon’s fate, wanted to
do Dillon justice. The gangers wanted to pumsh
Shovey.
In some way ot other a feW of the gangers
commanded money, and Sent the girl OVer to
Van Diemen s Land.
“ And there, one dav in Campbell-street, she
accused Shovey of being at large on a false certi-
ficate. She would not leave him till she procured
his arrest then and there.
* * *
“He was brought upon suspicion of being
an absconder.
“He was stripped to his waist in court—the
woman was present—and his ‘marks’ compared
with the certificate. ‘ They tallied fairly,’ the
magistrate said.
“ Then the measuring-rod was brought, and his
height was taken. ‘lt was near enough,’ the
magistrate considered.
“ The woman asked the Bench to order a search-
ing of the Sydney Gazette. ‘The file was
defective,’pronounced the magistrate.
“‘What can I do to prove, your Worship, this
man is William Shovey, bolter—not William
Dillon, free by servitude? I’ll stake my life it is
him !’ cried the woman.
“ The Bench misunderstood—possibly.

gasped an echo of his words: ‘ You—believe —

The Bulletin is excluded from Brisbane
Parliamentary Library. That it may be excluded
from all official and public libraries is the sincere
wish of its proprietary. The Queensland mem-
bers, including Griffith, read it from cover to
coven
A writer in the North American Review
suggested the formation of a Lawyers’ Guild for
the defence of the defenceless in police-courts.
The good young man type of lawyer who would
join such a guild is generally an idiot. It is not
uncommon to hear a prisoner defended by assigned
counsel exclaim to the latter in a loud whisper :
“Put for you I’d have got off ! ” It all reminds
us of the French story. Judge : “ What have you
to say why sentence,” &o. Prisoner : “ I can only
plead the youth of my counsel.”
Agents required everywhere throughout
Australasia. Secretaries of friendly societies,
Unions, &e., should write. Previous experience
unnecessary. Gcod pay and permanent positions.
For particulars, apply, the Co-Operative Irriga-
tion and Mercantile Society of Australia, Ltd.
49 Qileeh-street, Melbourne.

The amalgamation of the Exchange Bank
of Australia, Ltd., with the Sydney Deposit Bank,
King-street, is a step in the right direction and as
following in the line of the English banks, a great
number of whom are adopting this satisfactory
method of concentrating their energies and
capital. The total business of the Exchange
Bank has been merged into that of the Sydney
Deposit Bank, and under the able management of
Mr. T. S. Richardson, the General Manager, con-
tinued success is assured. The latest development
in banking has been promulgated by the Sydney
Deposit Bank who have for some time past given
practical effect to the principle of equitably-
dividing banker’s profits with depositors’. The
Exchange Department is very interesting, and
travellers to all parts of the world are informed
that they can obtain or dispose of coins and notes
of all nations, also circular notes of exchange are
issued negotiable in all the principal cities through-
out the world.*
The Relief of a Congested Labour Market—the
Co-operative Irrigation Colonies.
Secured Deposits. —ln view of the promised
legislation to regulate the business of those finan-
cial institutions which receive money on deposit
ftoin the public, we are pleased to notice that a
step in the right direction has been taken by the
Universal Land and Deposit Bank, Limited, who,
in a little pamphlet entitled “Mutual Banking,”
have called attention to a few points of practical
interest to all bank depositors. The main idea of
the above company appears to be that the
depositors, not the shareholders, should be the
first to receive the benefit of the profits made by
the bank, whilst, by the issue of debentures, they
(the depositors) obtain a first lien over all the
assets of the concern and are in possession of a
collateral security equal to the sum deposited,
which, by the way, bears interest. The scheme
is entirely novel, and we think the originators
deserve the support of the public in their efforts
to improve the position of the depositor. We
commend the above-mentioned pamphlet to the
attention of our readers who contemplate invest-
ing their money in this manner. It is forwarded
post free to any address by the bank, whose office
is at 301 Pitt-street, Sydney.*

knight, costs about £4O; “Ne Dan,” which is a
little higher, comes to £6O ; “ Yun Why,” or the
title of baron, is to be acquired for £2OO ; and for
£5OO you can be made “Chesoo,” or mayor of a
town with 10,000 inhabitants. “Ne Dan”
O'Connor would sound well and give pleasure to
all.

Betting as a Veterinary Art.
Ouk mare was Cissy. I tliink Cassandra was her
real name, but from Gassy it oame to Cissy and
sometimes Cis. You can get odds against a Cissy,
but if you enter Cassandra some idiots fancy the
prad to be a goer. But then we had won so many
little tenners and ponies that, even with nine
stone up, we never could get a decent bet after the
first fiver. I say WE, but I had no share, although
a great interest, in the mare. She belonged to my
only friends, friends who would lay me on and
stall me off at the will and the won't : and I make
my home with these simple people who know all
that is worth knowing about a horse.
Well, one Christmas there were races at the
Five Corners, not very small races either, but
good lumps (for the bush) of added money. Cissy
was in fine nick and the Trial Stakes was a fight
for her, but the added money was only £lO.
About a week before the races I got old Brickson
and his wife to listen to a proposal of mine. It
was this : —“ Now, Bob, attend to me. You can
make money if you follow my tip, and I do not
think it a swindle. It is a case of dog bite dog.
You run her in the Trial Stakes and win. That’s
a tenner. Scratch her for the big race and let her
go for the Shorts. I’H guarantee that you get
twenty to one against her, and you need only take
the ten pounds’ worth to win two centuries.”
Both Bob and Mrs. Brickson were afraid of
some cross work, but they knew me well, and I
refused to give them any information as to how I
would put Cissy back in the betting in a race for
which she would probably start at odds on. But at
last they said : “ Well, we’ll get that ten pounds,
and if I hear ten or five to one offered about her
for the Shorts, I’m there.” Then we said good
night, and dreamed of new clothing and an addi-
tional loose-box.
Christmas, eighteen hundred and somethingty-
somth. We don’t fly off to town. We go fishing
with the kids in the morning, after galloping
Cissy and Lucy and Fantan. In the evening we
sleep under the trees till it’s time to give the nags
a tub down and an hour’s walk. No headaches or
heartaches for us on Boxing Day, but a ride to the
Five Corners leading Cissy. We turned the other
two out in the garden. They were not wanted till
St. Patrick’s Day.
Cut it short, old man !
I must tell you we killed a sheep on Christmas
eve. Mind that.
Of course we won the Trial ; and, of course,
Brickson and his missus were annoyed at seeing
the big money taken by a duffer like M‘Guinn’s
Bluelight. Then oame a hullabaloo in the ring.
Cissy’s price? Even money. No more?
You’ll soon have to lay odds. But what
is this? A publican who had been look-
ing at Cissy suddenly shouts out, “I lay
ten to one Cissy.” Brickson and his wife are
at the scales weighing Tommy, our Tommy, who
is to ride. The lady snaps the offer, calls a
witness, and books it.' Then two bookies run
over to where I am holding the mare, at whose
forefeet is a pool of blood, and more dropping from
her nostrils. Back they go ! Twenty to one
Cissy ! Brickson cops to the tune of a tenner, and
comes to me to see what it all means. “Lord!
we’re done,” he said. Then I kicked his anile
sideways. “ All right, Bob ; that sheep bled well
the night before last.” But there was no more
betting and in ten minutes Herr Adalbert Hash,
the secretary, was proclaiming Cissy the winner
of the Shorts’ Open Sweepstakes.
One hotelkeeper and one bookmaker were heard
to say, “ a damned swindle.’’ Was it?
SCOTTY THE WRINKLER.
Flaubert’s particular friend was Brouilhet,
dramatist, living with a woman by whom he had
a son. When he was dying, he wanted to marry
her, but she refused, in order to inspire him with
hope that he would live on.
Says Sydney Echo: “N.S.W. Sydney
Bank depositors have a million and a quarter
sterling to them credit. But for strikes how much
more would it be?” Probably a million less.
But for past strikes the workman of to-day would
have absolutely nothing to save.
Alleged murderer Thomas, of Deniliquin
(N.S.W.) Gaol, had his little joke the other day.
He climbed to the top of a tank, 30ft. high, in the
gaol-yard, and peeped over the wall. A crowd
congregated, and Thomas proceeded to deliver am
oration suitable to the occasion. When asked to
come down, he said he thought he preferred
suicide in the tank. Archdeacon Holt, a gaol
visitor, crawled tremblingly up the ladder, bub
prisoner Thomas threatened to fall down and
break the parson’s neck. So the parson weakened.
Fresh ladders had to be got and placed so that a
warder was able to get on top of the tank and
above the crank, who was then standing on the
top ladder-rung, narrating how he had murdered
the only woman he ever loved and felt glad about
it. He had to be urged down the ladder by
having water “ cataraoted ” on him.

Flo. Bilton is billed at a London music-hall as “sister
to the Countess Clancarty.” Which reminds us that
Carlotta Patti once refused to sing in Manchester because
placarded as sister to Adelina.
Melba, according to London papers not up to her form
of two years ago, has been offered by Sir Gussy Harris,
£250 a night to sing at Covent Garden. She is re-engaged
for Paris Grand Opera and for Brussels in Sept.
Menie Muriel Dowie, the author of “ An English Girl in
Carpathians,” intends visiting Australia next year.
H. M. Stanley's accident is said to be the very latest
cheap ad. of R, S. Smythe. Archibald Forbes sprained
his ankle when he went to dine at Government
House, Sydney, and had to lecture sitting in a chair.
Wybert Reeve’s Reminiscences are out, in book-form,
Australian published. Very good they are, too, mostly
collocated from his papers in various journals. Once we
found a little blue-book about the Stratford Shakespeare
Centenary, 1861, in Melbourne Public Library, “Pre-
sented by William Creswick,” and it puzzled us what was
the little axe to grind till we found that the star was
“Mr. William Creswick, from London.” A trifle of this,
you know', in the “ Only Fosco,” but Wybert is an honour
to the profession.
Ibsen does not believe in patriotism. It is to him, a
thorough cosmopolitan, merely a racial superstition.
Ibsen, by the way, is more closely related to the Scotch
Covenanters than the Vikings.
David Christie Murray’s play, “Chums,” will be staged
at London Globe on August 27. David plays the lead.
Edmund Leathes, a stage lover who suffered from a
rather “nut-crackers” arrangement of nose and chin,
died the other day. He was the son of Doctor Donaldson
(a learned, bat forgotten person), and may be remem-
bered by many Australians as the Laertes in the first
Lyceum production of “Hamlet.” It was Leathes, too,
who tried to “ Led Astray ” the gigantic Helen Barry,
when Boucicault’s drama was played at the London
Gaiety.
“ Peril ” was first produced in London by the Bancrofts,
not -as a Melbourne paper muddles it—by the Kendals.
Moreover, the Kendals never ran the Prince of Wales.
Theatrical reminiscences need to be backed up by a good
memory.
A theatrical agent ran away from his English home
18 years ago. He hears now that he has been entitled to
£4OOO a year for several years past. It’s like a der-ream
or a pel-lay.
Chas. Warner’s “Drink,” at Drury, takes very well,
but critics think he has made the last act too revoltingly
real, and over the line of that artistic taste which
Franoisque Sarcey praised, when he said Warner was
better than Gil Nara, of the original company in Paris.
The late Henry Edwards acted in five now extinct Melb.
theatres: Queen’s, Olympic, Old Royal, Haymarket, Old
Princess’, and he made two balloon ascents from Sydney,
returning once only just in time to act Eugene de Lorme,
in “ Love’s Sacrifice,” to Brooke’s Matthew Elmore, at the
Victoria.
Parkinson, a Goshenite member of the London County
Council, was prosecuted last June for libel, on a perform-
ance by Japanese automata, at the Aquarium. He said a
female automaton was indecent, searching for a butterfly-
in its clothes, and the automata were fetched into Court,
like the Tommy and Sally of our ventriloquists. Said
Judge Hawkins: “Perhaps we might find a place for the
lady on the bench,” a subtle-caustic hit at Lady Cole-
ridge.
Pall Mall Bijdoht seems to have been hoaxed by
Toole's composite Maori bath-photograph. But as the
shadows were thrown in antagonistic directions, the fake
got exposed.
Nugent Robertson, of Sydney, says Sara Bernhardt is
an artist of the second rank, and deplores her sameness
in all parts. Nugent has never yet got over that
Athenseum Club fire.
Kowalski is about to travel for his health, and the
Sydney Choral and Orchestral Society intend to benefit
him.
According to an American paper one of the “ Evan-
geline ” girls was so anxious to come to Australia that she
‘ popped ” her diamonds to raise the passage-money. The
fame of “ Joe” MacMahon s moustache has wrecked many
a happy home, but he can’t help that.
Laura Villiera brings her mother, and Gwendolen Floyd
brings hers.
Dear Bulletin',—ln the middle of last winter I started
one Saturday morning to go to the Victoria Market, Mel-
bourne, by the first train, which leaves Essendon at 5.20
a.m. I reached Moonee Ponds station ere the train had
gone down to Essendon, and was standing on the up plat-
form when it came from Melbourne. It was pitch-dark
and there was no moon. The only light was from one or
two feeble gas-lamps on the platform. As the train
stopped I was exactly opposite a first-class carriage, which
was lighted up. It was one of the old stuffy sort, uphol-
stered m blue cloth and with blue curtains to the win-
uows. Only one compartment was occupied, and that
one by a man and a woman. They had carefully drawn

George Byrne, one of the oldest Brisbane residents,
died at Bowen Hdls on the last of July, aged 73. He
owned the Opera House and other valuable Queen-street
properties, and was as parsimonious as he was wealthy.
George Darrell notifies that lie is off to England with an
Australian Co. to play Australian authors - plural.
~ erret writes that he saw an audacious robbery at a
theatre not long ago. The checktaker in one
ot the upper-circles gave his tickets to a man in the
audience, and this fellow took them down to the paybox
to be resold, wherefore the swindle remunerated three.
Sara Bernhardt produces Giacosa’s “ Dame di Challant,”
a drama in five acts, the property of Grau, on her return
to America.
According to Fred. B. Norton’s prescription, “the
elevator may be taken for all theatrical ailments, before
and after meals, with almost immediate benefit.
Dalys Theatre in New York was originally Banvard’s
Museum, where was given a representation of the ortho-
dox and old-fashioned hell, with as many of the tradi-
tional horrors of eternal punishment as could be depicted
m the space at command. Beal flames broke out here and

A bashing act recently eventuated on the wharf of a
northern town between the manager ot a travelling
dramatic company and a local publican, who claimed to
have lent him money. They fell off the gangway into a
lighter where the conflict was renewed until they were
separated. But the boniface didn’t collect, and the
steamer left him blast-venting.

Staying at Narrabeen, George Leitch, divided his
attention between duck-shooting and fishing and
bathing and returned to shore to find his clothes rapidly
disappearing by the aid of two toughs. Putting his
gun to his bare and shiverimr shoulder, he called on them
to ‘ Stand and deliver,” and thus got possession of
clotnes, money, watch and chain. But if they d only
known it wasn’t loaded !

“ The Policeman,” by Edith Padey, is a
strange kind of novelette. Sergeant Courtney, in
a Sydney suburb, has a delightful small wife and
/A/r 6 £ lu,lly ’ when au attractive grass widow
(Mrs. Starbuck) appears on the scene as landlady
of the Liver and Lights Hotel. She buys a£l
ticket in Baker's Sweep, Melbourne Cup, but it
draws Moouraker, at 100 to 1, wherefore she per-
suades Courtney to relieve her of the ticket. All
Courtneys belongings are Wesleyanio, and the
ticket is a little rift which separates him from his
wife. Moouraker wins, and Courtney, in mere
pity, buys the mortgage on Mrs. Starbuck’s hotel.
Then she dies suddenly, under awful circum-
stances, and Courtney is ruined—henoe the moral.

alter your opinion?” “Not in the slightest.”
rapped out the witness.

Information,
Mr. J. T. Annear, M.L.A.
SENIOR MEMBER FOR MARYBOROUGH, (Q.)
Mb. J. T. Anneab, M.L. A., senior member for
Maryborough (Q.), was born at Budock Penryn,
Cornwall, England, on June 3, 1842, and was
educated at the Church of England Denomi-
national School, Budock. He arrived in Queens-
land in 1863, and settled in Ipswich where he
carried on contracting until 1867 when he went to
Maryborough where he was successful in obtain-
ing contracts for many important buildings,
including the new public offices and the State-
school. He was a member of the firm of J. T.
Annear and Co., railway contractors, who carried
out the Maryborough and Gympie x-ailway. Mr.
Annear was an alderman of Maryborough for 14
years, and was elected Mayor of the town for one
year He was first elected in 1883 to the House
of Assembly, and in 1887 he was elected the
second time to the House of Parliament as senior
member for Maryborough. In 1889 Mr. Annear paid
a visit to England and America, accompanied by
two well-known Australians, one a Minister for
Justice in a late N.S.W. Government. Mr.
Annear visited Washington, and had interviews
with President Harrison and Mr. Blaine, princi-
pally on legislative topics as affecting Australia
and the United States. On Mr. Annear’s arrival
in England he was heartily welcomed by many of
his old friends and relatives, including his parents,
who are still hale and hearty. On returning to
Queensland, he re-occnpied his seat in the House
of Assembly on the 18th September, 1889, and
then sat on the Opposition benches in support of
Sir Samuel Griffith’s policy. Shortly after
returning to his adopted land he visited his
constituents at Maryborough and received a
hearty and enthusiastic reception. He is senior
member of the firm of Cowly and Annear, Bris-
bane, who deal largely in cement and contractors’
supplies, which they import direct from Great
Britain and America. When the Morehead
Government went out of office and was replaced by
the Coalition Party, Mr. Annear was unanimously
elected Ministerial whip. In this capacity he
has proved a most pronounced success.
Jf the French fleet participates in a naval
demonstration with the English fleet, at Ports-
mouth, we may reasonably expect that both lots
of unwieldy monsters will manage to ram, shove,
and crash one another to the bottom of the sea.
Should Alfred of Edinburgh loom large in the
navigation we will hope for the worst. He can be
guaranteed to collide with any ironclad, foreign
or British, from a mile off.
A M.L. Labour-member before his election
used to wear a blue blouse and an ancient billy-
cock, but on his return a committee was formed to
provide him with another suit. Next day, he
pranced around in a black frock-coat and a stove-
pipe hat, whereupon the chairman of his
committee: —“Say, old man, we gave you those
swell togs to wear in Wellington; your old blue
blouse is good enough for us.” So the duds were
laid up in lavender until the legislator got to the
seat of Government.
Victorian Treasurer Gillies’ system of
what he called “ recoups,” is fast driving his suc-
cessor mad. Gillies’ finance was pecxxliar, and
ought to have been put in a museum. He used to
provide in his estimates for a sum of £20,000 to be
advanced out of the consolidated revenue to build
something or another, £17,500 to be afterwards
recouped out of the sale of something else, and
£6500 to be recouped from some other source, leav-
ing a credit of £4OOO, which would be advanced
along with £6OOO more—to be afterwards partly
recouped by disposing of a gaol somewhere—to
provide export-bonuses on butter or something.
The amount of the butter bonuses which weren’t
recouped out of the sale of the disused gaol, would
be recovered from the Stamp Department, and
anything that was recovered from the Stamp
Department, over and above the butter bonuses,
would be advanced as an addition to the charitable
vote, until it could be squared by the recovery of
an outstanding balance from the Mines Office.
Gillies seemed to keep most of the public cash in
small sums made up in brown paper, and to pre-
serve his accounts on the backs of old letters and
envelopes. When 2s. 2d. was wanted for pur-
poses of secondary education he had to grope
about his office till he found the identical ss. 9£d.
that had been paid in as fines for selling bad
flounders, because that particular 2s. 2d. was to
go towards making up 7s. 3d. for an office ruler,
and as these sums had all been “earmarked ” he
had to use them and no others. If he couldn’t
find them he would pay somebody 10s. fid. to help
him hunt for them, and “recoup ” that with one-
third of another amount received from the Lands
Department, and two-fifths of something else
which would also be carefully laid aside for the
purpose. He was a dreadfully conscientious old
Scotchman about the cash, but somehow he wasn’t
a success all the same.

It was when the flood was about that a
Melbourne journal made this warning remark—
“ Though the forenoon has been free of rain we
need not hollow before we are out of the wood.”
Now, only a woodpecker or a worm would be
guilty of hollowing in the wood.
Madame Bodichon, who died in England a
few weeks ago, was a remarkable English lady.
At her instance vast miasmatic tracts of Algeria
were planted with eucalyptus, Australian blue
gum, which made them healthy. She was
very wealthy, her husband a distinguished
Frenchman. Madame also figured as a first-class
water-colour artist, but was more eminent as a
founder of Girton College, and as the author of
the English legislation on married women’s
property.
The hanging of Fatta Chand, or, more
probably, the murder of his mate, has had an
effect upon the immigration of Hindoos to Mel-
bourne. Only 24 have landed in Victoria this
year, as against 765 in 1887.
Fourteen million Venetian despatches*
stored in the archives, are being overhauled,
dating from 883 a.d. Some are found from the
Venetian ambassadors, Paris and London, Queen
Elizabeth’s time, prophesying the defeat of the
Spanish Armada.

Hiram Pease (U.S.) lately died His in-
tended self-written epitaph :
Beneath this sod, and under these trees,
Lies all that is left of Hiram Pease :
He is not here —it’s only his pod—
His soul has shelled out and gone to God.
His executors sub-edited it; —“Here lies the
pod; the Pease are in Heaven.” Let us rhyme it
up again :
The pod is here beneath the sod,
The Pease are shelled and gone to God.

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The llulque Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper,
“The Bulletin,”
Premier A ustralian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Kepublican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces —-
Rel igious Interference in Polities.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over wfliom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official— that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. P. Kuoelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting array until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints, of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as. ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smythe, J.P.
